Interpretation of the Phrase 'by its Possibility or by its Origination'
The commentator clarifies the intended meaning of the dual proof for the Creator's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator reinterprets the phrase 'by its possibility or by its origination' to require origination as a condition or part, and why mere possibility is not sufficient for the mutakallimun.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun prove the Creator by origination, not mere possibility.

This lesson examines a crucial interpretive move by the commentator (al-Shārīḥ) regarding the first maslak of the mutakallimun. The phrase 'either by its possibility or by its origination' might be read as allowing proof by possibility alone, but that would contradict the majority position. The commentator resolves this by explaining that 'by its possibility' actually means possibility together with origination as a condition, while 'by its origination' stands alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to get the gist of the commentator's argument.
Pay attention to the initial apparent meaning and how it is corrected.
2
Identify the apparent meaning
Notice that the phrase 'by its possibility or by its origination' could be interpreted as inference by possibility alone.
This is the 'immediate understanding' mentioned in the text.
3
Understand the commentator's reinterpretation
See how the commentator splits the phrase: 'by its possibility' includes origination as a condition or part, while 'by its origination' stands alone.
The word 'based on' relates to the whole disjunctive phrase.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Why does this matter for the mutakallimun's methodology? The majority require origination as the genuine cause of need.
This lesson shows that mere possibility is not accepted as a proof by the mutakallimun.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إما بإمكانه أو بحدوثه المتبادر من هذه العبارة ومن سياق كلامه فيما بعد هو الاستدلال بالإمكان وحده ولما كان مخالفاً لمذهب جمهور المتكلمين وقد قيد المسلك بكونه للمتكلمين، ولم يسبق من المصنف تعرض لقول بعضهم بأن علة الاحتياج مجرد الإمكان لم يلتفت إليه الشارح، وحمل قوله إما بإمكانه على اعتبار الإمكان مع اعتبار الحدوث شرطاً أو شطراً، وقوله : أو بحدوثه على اعتبار الحدوث وحده فقوله بناء متعلق لمجموع قوله إما بإمكانه أو بحدوثه وجعله متعلقاً بالحدوث فقط حتى يتجه حمل الإمكان على الإمكان المجرد بعيد جداً، والحق أنه لو لم يقيد المسلك الأول بذلك كما لا يقيد في المحصل لكان أظهر.
His saying: (either by its possibility or by its origination) the immediate understanding from this expression and from the context of his words afterward is inference by possibility alone. But since this contradicts the position of the majority of the mutakallimun, and he restricted the way to being for the mutakallimun, and the author had not previously mentioned the statement of some that the cause of need is mere possibility, the commentator ignored it. He interpreted his saying 'by its possibility' as considering possibility together with considering origination as a condition or part, and his saying 'by its origination' as considering origination alone. So his word 'based on' relates to the totality of his saying 'either by its possibility or by its origination', and making it relate only to origination so as to direct possibility to absolute possibility is far-fetched. The truth is that if he had not restricted the first way with that, as it is not restricted in al-Muhassal, it would have been clearer.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage is a clarification by the commentator on the meaning of the phrase 'إما بإمكانه أو بحدوثه' (either by its possibility or by its origination) from the first maslak (way) of the mutakallimūn. The immediate reading would suggest that one can prove the Creator by considering possibility alone, but this contradicts the majority position of the mutakallimūn, who hold that origination is the genuine cause of need (ʿillat al-ḥājah). Since the author restricted this first maslak to the mutakallimūn, the commentator reinterprets: 'by its possibility' actually means possibility together with origination as a condition (sharṭ) or part (shāṭr), while 'by its origination' means origination alone. The word 'based on' (bināʾ) in the original text is thus linked to the entire disjunctive phrase, not just to origination. If the intended meaning were to take possibility alone, that would be far-fetched. The commentator notes that had the author not restricted the first way in this manner—as it is not restricted in al-Muḥaṣṣal by Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī—the meaning would have been clearer. This lesson highlights a key methodological point: the mutakallimūn do not accept mere possibility as a proof; they require origination.

Key Terms
إمكان
حدوث
علة الاحتياج
المسلك الأول
الشارح
المحصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun prove the existence of the Creator by the mere possibility of the world.
Correction: The mutakallimun require origination (ḥudūth) either alone or as a condition for possibility; they do not accept mere possibility as a proof.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator (al-Shārīḥ) interpret the phrase 'إما بإمكانه أو بحدوثه' to mean?
A) Inference by possibility alone
B) Inference by possibility with origination as a condition, or by origination alone
C) Inference by origination alone
D) Inference by both together

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun do not rely on mere possibility to prove the Creator; they require origination as a condition or as the sole proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate the proofs for God's existence, do you content yourself with the most common, shallow reasoning, or do you dig deeper into the precise arguments the scholars have articulated? What does your approach say about your commitment to truth?
?
How often do you rush to interpret a theological text based on its surface meaning, without considering the deeper context or the positions of the majority of scholars? Are you willing to set aside your first impression and embrace a more rigorous understanding, even if it humbles you?

Proof of the Eternal Creator from Temporality
First proof of the mutakallimūn for the existence of an eternal creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the first proof of the mutakallimūn for the existence of an eternal creator based on the temporality of bodies and accidents.

Core Idea
Everything temporal needs a cause; that cause must be eternal.

This passage presents the foundational argument used by the mutakallimūn to establish the existence of God. It begins with observable phenomena—bodies and their accidents—and through logical reasoning arrives at the necessity of an eternal creator. Let's examine this tightly reasoned proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the concise style of the argument.
The argument is presented in a single sentence in the original.
2
Identify the premises
The argument assumes that bodies and accidents are temporally originated (ḥāditha). Understand that this is taken as already proven.
This is a crucial assumption from earlier discussions.
3
Follow the logical chain
From the premises, the argument concludes a maker is necessary. Then it eliminates the possibility that the maker itself is temporal by showing it would lead to circularity or infinite regress.
Circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul) are considered impossible in kalām.
4
Understand the conclusion
The only remaining possibility is that the maker is eternal (qadīm). Recognize that this is exactly what the argument sought to prove.
The argument not only proves a creator but specifies the creator must be eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الآثار على وجود المؤثر فقالوا : ( إن الأجسام محدثة لما مر فكذا الأعراض فلا بد لها من صانع ولا يكون حادثا وإلا احتاج إلى مؤثر آخر فيلزم الدور أو التسلسل أو الانتهاء إلى قديم والأولان باطلان والثالث هو المطلوب.
The traces indicate the existence of an effect. So they said: 'Bodies are temporally originated as previously stated, and likewise accidents, so they must have a maker. And that maker cannot be temporally originated, otherwise it would need another effect, leading to either circularity or infinite regress or ending at an eternal. The first two are invalid, and the third is what is sought.'
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (maslak) of the mutakallimūn for establishing the existence of a creator. It begins by stating the evidence from effects (al-āthār) pointing to an effective cause (al-mu'aththir). The proof proceeds: bodies are temporally originated (ḥāditha) as previously proven, and accidents (a'rāḍ) are likewise temporally originated. Therefore, they require a maker (ṣāniʻ). That maker itself cannot be temporally originated because if it were, it would need another effect, leading to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul) or ultimately ending at an eternal being (qadīm). Circularity and infinite regress are false, so the only remaining possibility is that the maker is eternal, which is the desired conclusion.

Key Terms
المسلك الأول
الآثار
المؤثر
الأجسام
الأعراض
حادث
قديم
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only proves a creator, not that the creator is eternal.
Correction: The argument explicitly eliminates any temporal cause and concludes the creator must be eternal (qadīm).

Checkpoint
What does the first argument conclude about the creator?
A) The creator is temporally originated
B) The creator is eternal
C) The creator is impossible
D) The creator is unknown

Key Takeaway
The first proof of the mutakallimūn demonstrates that an eternal creator is necessary to avoid logical absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that the Creator is eternal and not bound by time, how does that change the way you rely on Him in your daily struggles? Do you ever behave as if God is like a powerful human who is limited by time?
?
The proof eliminates the possibility of an infinite chain of temporal causes. In your own life, do you ever place your ultimate trust in things that are themselves temporal (like wealth, status, or relationships) as if they were eternal? What would it mean for you to anchor your hope only in the Eternal?

The Mutakallimūn's Argument from Temporality and the Need for Possibility
The commentator explains the common approach of mutakallimūn and the need to supplement the temporality argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument from temporality (ḥudūth) leads to an eternal being but does not prove necessity, and why the argument from possibility (imkān) is needed to complete the proof.

Core Idea
Temporality proves an eternal maker; possibility proves that maker is necessary.

Sanusi's commentary clarifies the standard approach of the mutakallimūn. While they are known for the argument from temporality, this passage shows that argument alone does not establish necessity. The commentator invites us to reflect on how the possibility argument fills the gap.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two arguments
Read the Arabic passage once and note the mention of both ḥudūth (temporality) and imkān (possibility). The commentator explains their roles.
2
Understand the limitation of ḥudūth
The commentator states that the ḥudūth argument ends at an eternal being (qadīm) but not necessarily a necessary being (wājib). Reflect on why this is insufficient for proving God's necessary existence.
The ḥudūth argument shows that the world must have a beginning and thus a creator who is eternal, but that creator could be contingent in itself.
3
Recognize the need for imkān
The commentator says that proving necessity requires the argument from possibility. Consider why the possibility argument is suited to establish necessity.
The possibility argument argues that contingent beings require a necessary being to avoid infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والمشهور أن المتكلمين) أي ليس في كلامهم الاستدلال بالإمكان بل بالحدوث، والمراد من الأحوال الحدوث والجمع باعتبار الخصوصيات لكن اللازم من هذا المشهور أعني مسلك الحدوث هو الانتهاء إلى القديم فيحتاج إلى الاستدلال على أن ذلك القديم واجب والظاهر أن ذلك إنما هو بمسلك الإمكان فتدبر. عين قوله : ( ولا يكون حادثا وإلا احتاج إلخ ) في هذا التقرير نوع ركاكة لأن حاصله أن ذلك الصانع إن كان حادثاً يلزم إما أحد المحالين، وإما المطلوب، ولا شك أن لزوم المطلوب ليس بمحذور من حيث هو كذلك فلا يستقيم الاستدلال ببطلان اللازم على بطلان الملزوم فلا يثبت قوله ولا يكون حادثا فالأظهر أن نقول : والثالث خلف باطل مع أنه المطلوب أو نقول وذلك الصانع إما حادث أو قديم ، والأول باطل للزوم الدور أو التسلسل . والثاني هو المطلوب .
His statement: 'It is well-known that the mutakallimūn' means that in their discourse they do not use the argument from possibility but from temporality. And what is meant by 'states' is temporality and the plural by consideration of particularities. But the necessary consequence of this well-known approach, meaning the approach of temporality, is ending at an eternal being. So one needs to prove that that eternal being is necessary. And it appears that this is only by the approach of possibility, so reflect. Also, his statement: 'And it is not temporally originated, otherwise it would need...' In this formulation there is a kind of weakness because its summary is that if that maker is temporally originated, then either one of the two impossibilities would follow, or the sought outcome. And there is no doubt that the occurrence of the sought outcome is not a fault as such, so the reasoning by the falsity of the necessary for the falsity of the necessitator is not sound. So his statement that it is not temporally originated is not established. The clearer thing is to say: The third is an invalid concurrent but is the sought outcome; or to say: That maker is either temporally originated or eternal. The first is false due to circularity or infinite regress, and the second is the sought outcome.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (taʻlīq) on the first maslak. The commentator explains that the common understanding is that the mutakallimūn rely on the argument from temporality (ḥudūth) rather than possibility (imkān). He notes that the approach of temporality inevitably ends at an eternal being (qadīm), but one must still prove that this eternal being is necessary (wājib), which is usually done through the approach of possibility. Then he critiques the specific phrasing 'wa lā yakūnu ḥādithan' (and it is not temporally originated). He points out a logical weakness: the original argument states that if the maker were temporally originated, it would lead either to an impossibility (circularity or regress) or to the desired conclusion. But concluding that something is not the case because an alternative leads to an impossibility is valid only if the impossibility is exclusive. Here, since one of the alternatives is the desired conclusion, the implication is not a contradiction. The commentator offers two improved formulations: either state that the third alternative (the maker being eternal) is the only valid one, or directly posit the disjunction that the maker is either temporally originated or eternal, with the first being false and the second being the sought outcome.

Key Terms
المتكلمين
الاستدلال بالإمكان
الحدوث
القديم
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from temporality (ḥudūth) alone proves that God is necessary.
Correction: The ḥudūth argument only establishes an eternal being; necessity is proven by the argument from possibility (imkān).

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say 'so reflect' at the end of this passage?
A) Because the ḥudūth argument is flawed and should be abandoned.
B) Because the ḥudūth argument only leads to an eternal being, not a necessary one, and one must reflect on how to prove necessity.
C) Because the mutakallimūn avoid using the argument from possibility.
D) Because the passage is unclear and needs further reflection.

Key Takeaway
The argument from temporality reaches an eternal maker, but only the argument from possibility proves that maker is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the evidence for God’s existence, do you stop at the fact that the world had a beginning, or do you press on to affirm that God is necessary—the one who must exist? How would your trust in Him deepen if you truly believed He cannot not exist?
?
The commentator says to 'reflect' on the insufficiency of the temporality argument alone. What personal reflection does this provoke in you about your own intellectual journey: have you ever settled for a superficial proof without grasping the deeper necessity of God? What would it take for you to make that reflection a reality in your daily conviction?
?
Imagine your faith was based only on the temporality argument—knowing God is eternal but not necessarily existing. Would your worship be different than it is now? How does affirming God’s necessity (that He must exist) change the way you rely on Him in moments of distress or doubt?

Refining the Logical Formulation of the First Argument
The commentator critiques a specific formulation and offers two improved versions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical weakness in the original formulation of the first argument and learn two improved formulations.

Core Idea
A valid proof by contradiction requires that all consequences of the false assumption be impossible.

The commentator continues his refinement of the first maslak by critiquing a specific phrasing. He points out that the original conditional argument does not logically force the negation of temporality because one of the alternatives is the desired conclusion itself. He then offers two clearer formulations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the original phrasing
Read the Arabic passage and locate the phrase 'wa lā yakūnu ḥādithan' (and it is not temporally originated). This is the target of the critique.
2
Understand the logical weakness
The original argument posits that if the maker were temporally originated, it would lead either to an impossibility (circularity/regress) or to the desired conclusion (that it is eternal). The problem is that the desired conclusion is not impossible, so the implication does not force the negation of temporality.
For a proof by contradiction to work, all consequences must be impossible. Here, one consequence is the sought outcome, which is not impossible.
3
Learn the first improved formulation
The commentator suggests saying: 'The third is an invalid concurrent but is the sought outcome.' This rephrases the argument to avoid the logical flaw.
This formulation explicitly states that the third alternative (eternal) is a false concurrent, meaning it is not actually a consequence, but it is the desired conclusion.
4
Learn the second improved formulation
The commentator offers: 'The maker is either temporally originated or eternal. The first is false due to circularity or infinite regress, so the second is the sought outcome.' This is a direct disjunctive syllogism.
This formulation is clearer because it presents a simple disjunction and eliminates the false alternative, leaving the desired conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والمشهور أن المتكلمين) أي ليس في كلامهم الاستدلال بالإمكان بل بالحدوث، والمراد من الأحوال الحدوث والجمع باعتبار الخصوصيات لكن اللازم من هذا المشهور أعني مسلك الحدوث هو الانتهاء إلى القديم فيحتاج إلى الاستدلال على أن ذلك القديم واجب والظاهر أن ذلك إنما هو بمسلك الإمكان فتدبر. عين قوله : ( ولا يكون حادثا وإلا احتاج إلخ ) في هذا التقرير نوع ركاكة لأن حاصله أن ذلك الصانع إن كان حادثاً يلزم إما أحد المحالين، وإما المطلوب، ولا شك أن لزوم المطلوب ليس بمحذور من حيث هو كذلك فلا يستقيم الاستدلال ببطلان اللازم على بطلان الملزوم فلا يثبت قوله ولا يكون حادثا فالأظهر أن نقول : والثالث خلف باطل مع أنه المطلوب أو نقول وذلك الصانع إما حادث أو قديم ، والأول باطل للزوم الدور أو التسلسل . والثاني هو المطلوب .
His statement: 'It is well-known that the mutakallimūn' means that in their discourse they do not use the argument from possibility but from temporality. And what is meant by 'states' is temporality and the plural by consideration of particularities. But the necessary consequence of this well-known approach, meaning the approach of temporality, is ending at an eternal being. So one needs to prove that that eternal being is necessary. And it appears that this is only by the approach of possibility, so reflect. Also, his statement: 'And it is not temporally originated, otherwise it would need...' In this formulation there is a kind of weakness because its summary is that if that maker is temporally originated, then either one of the two impossibilities would follow, or the sought outcome. And there is no doubt that the occurrence of the sought outcome is not a fault as such, so the reasoning by the falsity of the necessary for the falsity of the necessitator is not sound. So his statement that it is not temporally originated is not established. The clearer thing is to say: The third is an invalid concurrent but is the sought outcome; or to say: That maker is either temporally originated or eternal. The first is false due to circularity or infinite regress, and the second is the sought outcome.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (taʻlīq) on the first maslak. The commentator explains that the common understanding is that the mutakallimūn rely on the argument from temporality (ḥudūth) rather than possibility (imkān). He notes that the approach of temporality inevitably ends at an eternal being (qadīm), but one must still prove that this eternal being is necessary (wājib), which is usually done through the approach of possibility. Then he critiques the specific phrasing 'wa lā yakūnu ḥādithan' (and it is not temporally originated). He points out a logical weakness: the original argument states that if the maker were temporally originated, it would lead either to an impossibility (circularity or regress) or to the desired conclusion. But concluding that something is not the case because an alternative leads to an impossibility is valid only if the impossibility is exclusive. Here, since one of the alternatives is the desired conclusion, the implication is not a contradiction. The commentator offers two improved formulations: either state that the third alternative (the maker being eternal) is the only valid one, or directly posit the disjunction that the maker is either temporally originated or eternal, with the first being false and the second being the sought outcome.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a false assumption leads to the desired conclusion, then the assumption must be false.
Correction: The desired conclusion is not an impossibility, so the implication does not force the falsehood of the assumption.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator consider the original formulation weak?
A) Because it uses a disjunction.
B) Because one of the alternatives is the sought outcome, which is not impossible.
C) Because it involves circular reasoning.
D) Because it denies the temporality of the maker.

Key Takeaway
A valid proof by contradiction requires that all consequences of the false assumption be impossible; if one consequence is the desired conclusion, the proof fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue for a belief you hold dear, do you ever dismiss evidence that would lead to an uncomfortable conclusion? How does this lesson's warning about treating a desired outcome as a contradiction challenge your own intellectual honesty? Commit to identifying one belief you hold that you might be defending with flawed logic.
?
The commentator points out that 'the sought outcome is not a fault'—meaning that wanting something to be true does not make it a valid proof. Reflect on a time when you convinced yourself of something simply because the conclusion was appealing. How can you train yourself to separate your desires from the validity of an argument? What specific step will you take this week to test a cherished belief with the same rigor shown here?
?
Is there an area of your life where you have assumed that because a certain path leads to a good result, the path itself must be correct? How does this lesson's logical critique expose a subtle form of self-deception? What would it cost you to honestly re-examine that assumption today?

The Commonality of Existence and the Need for Restriction
Discusses the problem of existence as a common attribute and proposed solutions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why absolute existence cannot distinguish God from other beings and how restriction to specific existence or to an attribute like wajub is required.

Core Idea
Absolute existence is common; distinguishing God requires a restricted attribute.

This passage tackles a fundamental problem in theological attribute discussions: if existence is shared by all beings, how can it be a distinctive attribute of God? The commentator explores possible solutions, including the idea that only specific existence or other intelligible attributes like wajub can serve as genuine distinguishers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Notice the flow: problem, exception, suggestion, solution.
2
Identify the core problem
Understand why absolute existence cannot be a distinguishing attribute: it is common (mushtarak) among all existents.
The passage mentions that it can only distinguish from non-existent possibles according to the Mu'tazila.
3
Examine the proposed solutions
The commentator suggests that existence in previous contexts may mean specific existence, and that a better approach is to restrict by negating contradiction. Also, the true distinguisher should be intelligible, like wajub.
Pay attention to the phrase 'so reflect' (fa ta'ammal) – it invites deeper thought.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why specific existence is not intelligible in its particularity and why wajub (necessity) can be a suitable distinguishing attribute.
This ties back to the broader goal of making God knowable through attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (مشترك بين الموجودات فلا يحصل به التمييز عن جميع الذوات المماثلة له في الذاتية، أو إن حصل التمييز عن بعضها وهو ذوات المعدومات الممكنة الثابتة في حال العدم عند المعتزلة ومقصودهم بيان ما يمتاز عن جميع الذوات، وقد عرفت أن الحياة المذكورة أيضاً كذلك بقي أن يقال المشترك هو الوجود المطلق، ويجوز أن يراد بالوجود فيما سبق الوجود الخاص فلو صير إلى التقييد بمجرد نفي المنافاة من غير تعرض لاشتراك الوجود لكان أولى، ويمكن أن يقال مرادهم بيان الأمر الذي يميزه تعالى عن غيره ويكون معقولاً لنا حتى يمتاز به عندنا ووجوده الخاص لا يعقل من حيث خصوصيته وإنما يعقل بالمقيدات الكلية وإن كانت منحصرة في فرد كالوجوب، فتأمل .
His saying: (It is common among existents, so distinction from all essences similar to it in essentiality is not obtained by it, or if distinction from some is obtained — that is, the essences of non-existent possibles established in the state of non-existence according to the Mu'tazila — and their intent is to show what distinguishes Him from all essences. And you have known that the mentioned life is also likewise. It remains to be said that the common is absolute existence, and it is permissible that by existence in the previous context is meant specific existence, so if one were to resort to restriction by merely negating contradiction without addressing the commonality of existence, that would be better. And it can be said that their intent is to show the matter that distinguishes Him, exalted, from others and is intelligible to us so that He is distinguished for us. And His specific existence is not understood in terms of its specificity, but rather it is understood by universal restrictions even if they are confined to an individual like wajub. So reflect.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage examines the difficulty of using existence (wujud) as a distinguishing attribute, since existence is common (mushtarak) among all existents. Therefore, absolute existence cannot distinguish God from other essences that are similar in essentiality (dhatiyyah). It can only distinguish from some, such as the essences of non-existent possibles (ma'dumat mumkinah) that are established in non-existence according to the Mu'tazila. The commentator notes that the same issue applies to life mentioned earlier. He then suggests that perhaps 'existence' in previous contexts means specific existence (wujud khass), not absolute existence. A better approach would be to restrict the concept by simply negating incompatibility (nafy al-manafah) without focusing on commonality. Finally, he argues that the intended distinguisher should be something intelligible (ma'qul) for us, such as wajub, because God's specific existence is not understood in its particularity but through universal restrictions, even if they are unique to an individual. The passage ends with 'so reflect' (fa ta'ammal), indicating a point for deeper consideration.

الوجود المطلق
الوجود الخاص
التمييز
الذواتية
الموجودية المقيدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence itself is a distinguishing attribute of God.
Correction: Existence is common among all existents, so absolute existence cannot distinguish God. A restricted attribute like wajub is needed.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why cannot absolute existence alone distinguish God from other beings?
A) Because God does not have existence
B) Because existence is common to all existents
C) Because existence is a non-entity
D) Because existence is not an attribute

Key Takeaway
Absolute existence is a common concept and cannot distinguish God; a restricted attribute like wajub is needed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah exists', do you unconsciously imagine a kind of existence like your own? This passage shows that absolute existence cannot even distinguish God from other existents. How does this realization challenge your mental image of Allah? Will you actively replace vague thoughts with the attribute of absolute necessity (wajub) in your worship today?
?
The lesson points out that God's specific existence is not understood by us directly. Are you content to worship a God whose essence you cannot fully grasp, or does your heart demand a clear picture? How can the concept of 'universal restrictions' (like necessity) help you have a proper, reverent understanding without overstepping?
?
If absolute existence is common, then what truly distinguishes you as a believer from others? This lesson teaches that only a restricted attribute (like necessity) sets God apart. What attribute do you rely on to set yourself apart in Allah's sight? Is it a 'common' one like good deeds, or a 'restricted' one like sincere dependence on Him? Commit to focusing on that unique quality today.

The General Fallacy of Confusing Accident with Subject
Al-Jurjani introduces the fallacy and its importance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key fallacy of confusing the accident with the subject of attribution and why recognizing it is crucial for refuting theological doubts.

Core Idea
Confusing concept with referent is the root of many doubts.

Al-Jurjani begins by identifying a recurring logical fallacy that underlies many theological arguments: confusing the accident (ʿāriḍ) with the subject of attribution (maʿrūḍ). This fallacy is essentially the confusion between a concept and its external referent. By mastering this distinction, you can both refute false arguments and protect your own reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice how al-Jurjani presents the fallacy as a source of many doubts.
The passage uses a metaphor of a vigilant heart (قلب شيحان) to emphasize alertness.
2
Understand the fallacy definition
Identify what 'confusing the accident with the subject' means. The accident is the concept, the subject is the real thing. Confusing them leads to invalid arguments.
Al-Jurjani later clarifies this as confusing the concept with the referent.
3
Reflect on the metaphor
Consider why a 'vigilant heart' is needed to guard against this fallacy. How does this prepare you to refute doubts?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثاني: في أن ذاته تعالى مخالفة لسائر الذوات

( وهذه ) المغالطة أعني اشتباه العارض بالمعروض ) منشأ لكثير من الشبه ) في مواضع عديدة، فإذا انتبهت له أي لهذا المنشأ ووقفت علي حاله وكنت ذا قلب شيحان ) أي يقطان غيور على حرمه التي هي بنات فكره ) انجلت عليك ) تلك الشبه ) وقدرت ( على ) أن تغلط ) غيرك ( وأمنت ) من ( أن تغالط ) أنت ( منها ) أي من تلك الشبه (قولهم الوجود مشترك إذ تجزم به ونتردد في الخصوصيات فنقول المجزوم به مفهوم الوجود لا ما صدق عليه الوجود ) لجواز أن يكون ذلك المفهوم خارجاً عن حقائق أفراده المتخالفة، فلا تكون حقيقة الوجود أمراً واحداً مشتركاً مجزوماً به، ( والنزاع) إنما وقع (فيه) لا في مفهوم عارض لحقيقته ( ومنها قولهم الوجود زائد إذ نعقل الوجود دون الماهية كما في الواجب مثلاً ( وبالعكس)، أي نعقل الماهية دون الوجود كمافي المثلث فلا يكون الوجود عيناً ولا داخلا، (قلنا فيه ما تقدم من أن الزائد مفهومه لا حقيقته ( ومنها الوحدة عدمية وإلا تسلسل قلنا : ) اللازم من دليلكم على تقدير صحته أن يكون ( مفهوم الوحدة) عدمياً لا وجودياً، إذ حينئذ يلزم تسلسل الوحدات الوجودية إلى ما لا نهاية لها ) ولا يلزم ) هذا التسلسل ( فيما صدق عليه فإنه مختلف ( فبعضه وجودي وبعضه عدمي وبعضه زائد وبعضه نفس الماهية كما مرت إليه الإشارة في مباحث الوحدة ) ومنها الصفات زائدة على الذات وإلا لكان المفهوم من العلم ومن القدرة ومن الصفات الآخر شيئاً ( واحداً ) هو عين الذات ولا شبهة في استحالته ( قلنا يكون ما صدقا ) أي ما صدق ( عليه ) العلم والقدرة مثلاً ) واحداً وأما المفهوم (فلا يكون واحداً بل لكل منهما مفهوم على حدة ( وأمثال ذلك أكثر من أن تحصى ( فلنكتف بما ذكرنا إذ لا يخفى عليك حالها .

ثم قال الشارح:
قوله : ( أعني اشتباه العارض بالمعروض) الأنسب لما سبق أن يقول أعني اشتباه مفهوم الموضوع بما صدق عليه فكان الباعث على ما ذكره كون الشبه الموردة من اشتباه العارض بالمعروض.
قوله : (ذا قلب شيحان الشيحان بفتح الياء الغيور الحذر على حرمه استعارة للقلب الذي يدفع شين الشبه وعار الأغاليط عن بنات فكره .
قوله قلنا فيه ما تقدم فإن قلت حاصل الاستدلال أن يقول تعقل ماهية الوجود ولو عن الواجب بالكلية، فيكون زائداً عليه وحينئذ لا يتجه الجواب قلت : المعلوم في الصورة المفروضة هو الغفلة عن تعقل حقيقة الواجب بالكنه وعن تعقله من حيث أنه واجب بوجه مع الغفلة عن تعقله بوجه ما .
قوله : ( على تقدير صحته إشارة إلى المنع بجواز أن تكون وحدة الوحدة نفسها وبأنه لا يلزم من جواز وجود طبيعية وجود جميع أفرادها فجاز أن تكون الوحدة وجودية ووحدة الوحدة عدمية كما مر مثله مراراً .
The First Observation - Second Aim: On that His Essence is different from all other essences

This fallacy, I mean the confusion of the accident with the subject of attribution, is the source of many doubts in numerous places. So if you become aware of it—i.e., this origin—and know its state, and you have a vigilant heart (i.e., alert and protective of its sanctities which are the daughters of its thought), those doubts become clear to you, and you are able to refute others and are safe from being refuted by them. Among them is their saying: existence is common, for we are certain of it and hesitate about the particulars. So we say: That which is certain is the concept of existence, not the referent of existence, because it is possible that that concept is external to the realities of its differing individuals; thus the reality of existence is not a single common certain thing. The dispute is only about it, not about the concept that is accidental to its reality. Among them is their saying: existence is additional, for we conceive existence without essence, as in the Necessary example, and vice versa (i.e., we conceive essence without existence, as in the triangle), so existence is neither identical nor intrinsic. We said about it what preceded: the additional is its concept, not its reality. Among them is: unity is non-existential, otherwise there would be an infinite regress. We said: The necessary consequence of your proof, on the assumption of its validity, is that the concept of unity is non-existential, not existential, because then an infinite regress of existential units would follow. But this regress does not follow regarding the referent, for it is diverse: some is existential, some non-existential, some additional, some identical to the essence, as was indicated in the investigations of unity. Among them is: the attributes are additional to the essence, otherwise the concept of knowledge, power, and the other attributes would be one thing identical to the essence, and there is no doubt of its impossibility. We said: The referent (i.e., that which knowledge and power are true of) is one, but the concept is not one; rather, each has its own concept. And the like is more than can be enumerated, so let us suffice with what we have mentioned, since its state is not hidden from you.

Then the commentator said:
His statement: (I mean the confusion of the accident with the subject of attribution) – it would be more appropriate for what preceded to say: I mean the confusion of the concept of the subject with its referent, for the reason for what he mentioned is that the doubts cited arise from the confusion of the accident with the subject.
His statement: (vigilant heart) – “al-shiḥān” with a fatḥa on the yā’ means the one who is protective and cautious over his sanctities; it is a metaphor for the heart that repels the shame of doubt and the disgrace of fallacies from the daughters of its thought.
His statement: “We said about it what preceded” – If you say: The gist of the argument is that he says: one conceives the essence of existence even regarding the Necessary, so it would be additional to Him, and then the response would not be directed. I say: What is known in the supposed case is the neglect of conceiving the reality of the Necessary in its essence, and of conceiving it insofar as it is necessary in a certain manner, along with neglect of conceiving it in any manner.
His statement: (on the assumption of its validity) – An indication of the rebuttal by the possibility that the unity of unity is itself, and that the possibility of the existence of a nature does not necessitate the existence of all its individuals; so it is possible that unity is existential while the unity of unity is non-existential, as was similarly mentioned repeatedly.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of multiple doubts that arise from the fallacy of confusing the accident (ʿāriḍ) with the subject of attribution (maʿrūḍ). The commentator (al-Jurjani) begins by identifying this fallacy as the source of many doubts. He explains that if one understands this origin, one can refute these doubts and avoid being refuted. He then lists four specific doubts and answers them. The first doubt is that existence is common (mushtarak) because we are certain of its concept but hesitate in applying it to particulars. The answer: the certainty pertains to the concept (mafhūm) of existence, not its referent (mā ṣadaqa ʿalayhi). The concept may be external to the diverse realities of individuals, so the reality of existence is not a single common thing. The dispute is about the reality, not the concept. The second doubt: existence is additional (zāʾid) to essence because we can conceive existence without essence (e.g., the Necessary) and essence without existence (e.g., a triangle). The answer: what is additional is the concept, not the reality. The third doubt: unity is non-existential (ʿadamiyya), otherwise an infinite regress of units would follow. The answer: the regress would apply only to the concept of unity, not the referent, because the referent of unity is diverse—some existential, some non-existential, some additional, some identical to essence. The fourth doubt: the attributes are additional to the essence, otherwise the concepts of knowledge, power, etc. would be identical. The answer: the referent (i.e., what the attributes are true of) is one, but each attribute has a distinct concept. The commentator then explains each part of the matn: clarifying that the confusion is between concept and referent, explaining the metaphor of the vigilant heart, addressing a potential objection about the argument for the additional nature of existence, and noting that the rebuttal to the unity argument includes the possibility that the unity of unity itself may be non-existential.

Key Terms
اشتباه العارض بالمعروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fallacy is merely a minor logical error.
Correction: Al-Jurjani emphasizes it is the source of many major theological doubts.

Checkpoint
What does al-Jurjani identify as the source of many doubts?
A) The confusion between essence and existence
B) The confusion between the accident and the subject of attribution
C) The confusion between unity and multiplicity
D) The confusion between necessary and possible existence

Key Takeaway
Recognizing the confusion between concept and referent is essential for sound theological reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent theological doubt you encountered or entertained. Was it possibly rooted in confusing an abstract concept with the actual reality it describes? What would it take for you to trace that doubt back to its origin and dismantle it using Al-Jurjani’s insight? Commit to doing so before the end of this week.
?
The passage describes a 'vigilant heart' that guards the 'daughters of its thought'—the sound intellectual conclusions you hold. Are you actively guarding your beliefs from confusion, or have you allowed ambiguity to settle into areas like your understanding of God’s attributes or destiny? Name one belief you will re-examine today to ensure it is clear and not muddled by this fallacy.
?
Al-Jurjani says that recognizing this fallacy allows you to refute others and be safe from being refuted. When you engage in discussion about Islam, are you equipped to spot this confusion in arguments—both in others and in yourself? What specific step can you take this week to sharpen that skill?

First Doubt: Existence is Common (الوجود مشترك)
Al-Jurjani refutes the doubt that existence is a single common reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that 'existence is common' confuses the concept of existence with its referent, and how al-Jurjani refutes it.

Core Idea
Certainty about existence applies to the concept, not the reality.

A common doubt among theologians is that existence is a single shared reality because we are certain of it while differing about its particulars. Al-Jurjani shows that this argument confuses the concept of existence with its actual referent in individuals. The certainty pertains only to the concept, which may be external to the diverse realities of existing things.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt and answer
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Identify the claim: existence is common because we are certain of it but differ in particulars. Then notice the refutation: the certainty is about the concept, not the referent.
The key phrase is 'المجزوم به مفهوم الوجود لا ما صدق عليه'.
2
Understand the distinction
Grasp the difference between the concept (mafhūm) of existence and the referent (mā ṣadaqa ʿalayhi). The concept is a mental abstraction; the referent is the actual existence of each thing.
Al-Jurjani says the concept may be 'external to the realities of its differing individuals'.
3
Apply to other arguments
Reflect on how this same distinction can be used to refute other arguments that confuse concept with reality.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of multiple doubts that arise from the fallacy of confusing the accident (ʿāriḍ) with the subject of attribution (maʿrūḍ). The commentator (al-Jurjani) begins by identifying this fallacy as the source of many doubts. He explains that if one understands this origin, one can refute these doubts and avoid being refuted. He then lists four specific doubts and answers them. The first doubt is that existence is common (mushtarak) because we are certain of its concept but hesitate in applying it to particulars. The answer: the certainty pertains to the concept (mafhūm) of existence, not its referent (mā ṣadaqa ʿalayhi). The concept may be external to the diverse realities of individuals, so the reality of existence is not a single common thing. The dispute is about the reality, not the concept. The second doubt: existence is additional (zāʾid) to essence because we can conceive existence without essence (e.g., the Necessary) and essence without existence (e.g., a triangle). The answer: what is additional is the concept, not the reality. The third doubt: unity is non-existential (ʿadamiyya), otherwise an infinite regress of units would follow. The answer: the regress would apply only to the concept of unity, not the referent, because the referent of unity is diverse—some existential, some non-existential, some additional, some identical to essence. The fourth doubt: the attributes are additional to the essence, otherwise the concepts of knowledge, power, etc. would be identical. The answer: the referent (i.e., what the attributes are true of) is one, but each attribute has a distinct concept. The commentator then explains each part of the matn: clarifying that the confusion is between concept and referent, explaining the metaphor of the vigilant heart, addressing a potential objection about the argument for the additional nature of existence, and noting that the rebuttal to the unity argument includes the possibility that the unity of unity itself may be non-existential.

Key Terms
وجود مشترك
مفهوم
ما صدق عليه

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, what is the certain thing in the claim that existence is common?
A) The reality of existence
B) The concept of existence
C) Both concept and reality
D) Neither concept nor reality

Key Takeaway
The argument that existence is common fails because it confuses the certain concept with the diverse realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You feel certain of God's existence and attributes, but are you sure your conceptual understanding truly captures His reality, or do you risk reducing the Divine to your mental ideas? How will you guard against confusing concept with reality in your theology?
?
Have you ever insisted on a theological position simply because the concept seemed clear to you, without examining whether your certainty was about the concept or the actual reality? What will you do differently to check yourself?
?
If the very meaning of existence is not a single common reality for creatures, how much more cautious should you be when speaking of the existence of the Creator? Will you now pause before making absolute statements about matters beyond your conceptual grasp?

Second Doubt: Existence is Additional (الوجود زائد)
Al-Jurjani refutes the doubt that existence is additional to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's refutation of the argument that existence is additional to essence, based on the distinction between concept and reality.

Core Idea
What is additional is the concept, not the reality of existence.

Another objection claims existence must be additional to essence because we can conceive existence without essence (as in the case of God) and essence without existence (as in a triangle). Al-Jurjani responds by pointing out that what is additional is only the concept of existence, not its reality. The passage also addresses a potential counter-argument about conceiving God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt and answer
Read the Arabic text. The doubt: we can conceive existence without essence (e.g., God) and essence without existence (e.g., triangle). The answer: what is additional is the concept, not the reality.
Notice the phrase 'الزائد مفهومه لا حقيقته'.
2
Examine the implicit objection
In the commentary, an objection is raised: if one truly conceives God's essence without existence, then existence is additional. Al-Jurjani's answer explains that the supposed conception is actually a neglect of truly conceiving the essence.
The key point: we cannot fully conceive the reality of God's essence.
3
Reflect on the broader lesson
This distinction preserves the Islamic position that existence and essence are identical in God (the Necessary Being) while being distinct in contingent things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثاني: في أن ذاته تعالى مخالفة لسائر الذوات

( وهذه ) المغالطة أعني اشتباه العارض بالمعروض ) منشأ لكثير من الشبه ) في مواضع عديدة، فإذا انتبهت له أي لهذا المنشأ ووقفت علي حاله وكنت ذا قلب شيحان ) أي يقطان غيور على حرمه التي هي بنات فكره ) انجلت عليك ) تلك الشبه ) وقدرت ( على ) أن تغلط ) غيرك ( وأمنت ) من ( أن تغالط ) أنت ( منها ) أي من تلك الشبه (قولهم الوجود مشترك إذ تجزم به ونتردد في الخصوصيات فنقول المجزوم به مفهوم الوجود لا ما صدق عليه الوجود ) لجواز أن يكون ذلك المفهوم خارجاً عن حقائق أفراده المتخالفة، فلا تكون حقيقة الوجود أمراً واحداً مشتركاً مجزوماً به، ( والنزاع) إنما وقع (فيه) لا في مفهوم عارض لحقيقته ( ومنها قولهم الوجود زائد إذ نعقل الوجود دون الماهية كما في الواجب مثلاً ( وبالعكس)، أي نعقل الماهية دون الوجود كمافي المثلث فلا يكون الوجود عيناً ولا داخلا، (قلنا فيه ما تقدم من أن الزائد مفهومه لا حقيقته ( ومنها الوحدة عدمية وإلا تسلسل قلنا : ) اللازم من دليلكم على تقدير صحته أن يكون ( مفهوم الوحدة) عدمياً لا وجودياً، إذ حينئذ يلزم تسلسل الوحدات الوجودية إلى ما لا نهاية لها ) ولا يلزم ) هذا التسلسل ( فيما صدق عليه فإنه مختلف ( فبعضه وجودي وبعضه عدمي وبعضه زائد وبعضه نفس الماهية كما مرت إليه الإشارة في مباحث الوحدة ) ومنها الصفات زائدة على الذات وإلا لكان المفهوم من العلم ومن القدرة ومن الصفات الآخر شيئاً ( واحداً ) هو عين الذات ولا شبهة في استحالته ( قلنا يكون ما صدقا ) أي ما صدق ( عليه ) العلم والقدرة مثلاً ) واحداً وأما المفهوم (فلا يكون واحداً بل لكل منهما مفهوم على حدة ( وأمثال ذلك أكثر من أن تحصى ( فلنكتف بما ذكرنا إذ لا يخفى عليك حالها .

ثم قال الشارح:
قوله : ( أعني اشتباه العارض بالمعروض) الأنسب لما سبق أن يقول أعني اشتباه مفهوم الموضوع بما صدق عليه فكان الباعث على ما ذكره كون الشبه الموردة من اشتباه العارض بالمعروض.
قوله : (ذا قلب شيحان الشيحان بفتح الياء الغيور الحذر على حرمه استعارة للقلب الذي يدفع شين الشبه وعار الأغاليط عن بنات فكره .
قوله قلنا فيه ما تقدم فإن قلت حاصل الاستدلال أن يقول تعقل ماهية الوجود ولو عن الواجب بالكلية، فيكون زائداً عليه وحينئذ لا يتجه الجواب قلت : المعلوم في الصورة المفروضة هو الغفلة عن تعقل حقيقة الواجب بالكنه وعن تعقله من حيث أنه واجب بوجه مع الغفلة عن تعقله بوجه ما .
قوله : ( على تقدير صحته إشارة إلى المنع بجواز أن تكون وحدة الوحدة نفسها وبأنه لا يلزم من جواز وجود طبيعية وجود جميع أفرادها فجاز أن تكون الوحدة وجودية ووحدة الوحدة عدمية كما مر مثله مراراً .
The First Observation - Second Aim: On that His Essence is different from all other essences

This fallacy, I mean the confusion of the accident with the subject of attribution, is the source of many doubts in numerous places. So if you become aware of it—i.e., this origin—and know its state, and you have a vigilant heart (i.e., alert and protective of its sanctities which are the daughters of its thought), those doubts become clear to you, and you are able to refute others and are safe from being refuted by them. Among them is their saying: existence is common, for we are certain of it and hesitate about the particulars. So we say: That which is certain is the concept of existence, not the referent of existence, because it is possible that that concept is external to the realities of its differing individuals; thus the reality of existence is not a single common certain thing. The dispute is only about it, not about the concept that is accidental to its reality. Among them is their saying: existence is additional, for we conceive existence without essence, as in the Necessary example, and vice versa (i.e., we conceive essence without existence, as in the triangle), so existence is neither identical nor intrinsic. We said about it what preceded: the additional is its concept, not its reality. Among them is: unity is non-existential, otherwise there would be an infinite regress. We said: The necessary consequence of your proof, on the assumption of its validity, is that the concept of unity is non-existential, not existential, because then an infinite regress of existential units would follow. But this regress does not follow regarding the referent, for it is diverse: some is existential, some non-existential, some additional, some identical to the essence, as was indicated in the investigations of unity. Among them is: the attributes are additional to the essence, otherwise the concept of knowledge, power, and the other attributes would be one thing identical to the essence, and there is no doubt of its impossibility. We said: The referent (i.e., that which knowledge and power are true of) is one, but the concept is not one; rather, each has its own concept. And the like is more than can be enumerated, so let us suffice with what we have mentioned, since its state is not hidden from you.

Then the commentator said:
His statement: (I mean the confusion of the accident with the subject of attribution) – it would be more appropriate for what preceded to say: I mean the confusion of the concept of the subject with its referent, for the reason for what he mentioned is that the doubts cited arise from the confusion of the accident with the subject.
His statement: (vigilant heart) – “al-shiḥān” with a fatḥa on the yā’ means the one who is protective and cautious over his sanctities; it is a metaphor for the heart that repels the shame of doubt and the disgrace of fallacies from the daughters of its thought.
His statement: “We said about it what preceded” – If you say: The gist of the argument is that he says: one conceives the essence of existence even regarding the Necessary, so it would be additional to Him, and then the response would not be directed. I say: What is known in the supposed case is the neglect of conceiving the reality of the Necessary in its essence, and of conceiving it insofar as it is necessary in a certain manner, along with neglect of conceiving it in any manner.
His statement: (on the assumption of its validity) – An indication of the rebuttal by the possibility that the unity of unity is itself, and that the possibility of the existence of a nature does not necessitate the existence of all its individuals; so it is possible that unity is existential while the unity of unity is non-existential, as was similarly mentioned repeatedly.
Source pages p. 20

Third Doubt: Unity is Non-Existential (الوحدة عدمية)
Al-Jurjani refutes the doubt that unity must be non-existential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's refutation of the argument that unity must be non-existential to avoid infinite regress.

Core Idea
The regress applies only to the concept of unity, not its varied referents.

Some argue that unity cannot be an existing thing because that would lead to an infinite regress of unities. Al-Jurjani counters by distinguishing the concept of unity from its referent. The infinite regress only applies if we consider the concept as a real entity; in reality, unity is diverse—some existent, some non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt and answer
Read the Arabic: the doubt is that unity must be non-existential (ʿadamiyya) to avoid regress. The answer: the regress only follows if we consider the concept of unity.
Key phrase: 'اللازم من دليلكم على تقدير صحته أن يكون مفهوم الوحدة عدمياً'.
2
Understand the refutation's structure
Al-Jurjani concedes that if the proof were valid, it would only show that the concept of unity is non-existential. But the referent (mā ṣadaqa ʿalayhi) is diverse: some unity is existential (e.g., the unity of God), some non-existential (e.g., the unity of a privation).
The commentator adds that the unity of unity itself may be non-existential.
3
Reflect on the broader point
This shows the importance of not confusing the conceptual level with the real level. The same fallacy underlies many doubts.

Source Text

When you reflect on your own existence, is your unity with your own self just an abstract idea, or does it drive you to seek consistency in your faith and character? If you feel disconnected from the reality of tawhid, what step will you take today to bridge that gap?
?
The passage distinguishes between concept and referent. Are you content with having a 'concept' of Allah's oneness, or do you truly refer your entire being to Him as the only One worthy of fear, hope, and love? What would it mean for you to live as a 'referent' of tawhid?

Fourth Doubt: Attributes are Additional (الصفات زائدة)
Al-Jurjani refutes the doubt that attributes are additional to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's refutation of the argument that divine attributes must be additional to the essence to avoid conceptual collapse.

Core Idea
The referent of attributes is one, but their concepts are distinct.

A final doubt concerns the divine attributes: if they were identical to the essence, then the concepts of knowledge, power, etc. would all be the same, which is absurd. Al-Jurjani responds by distinguishing the referent (the essence) from the concepts: the referent is one, but the concepts are multiple and distinct. This preserves both divine simplicity and the multiplicity of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt and answer
Read the Arabic: the doubt says if attributes were not additional, then the concepts would be identical. The answer: the referent is one, but the concepts are distinct.
Key phrase: 'ما صدقا واحداً وأما المفهوم فلا يكون واحداً'.
2
Understand the implication
This answer upholds that God's essence is simple yet possesses multiple attributes. The attributes are additional in concept but not in reality.
The commentator clarifies that it is the concepts that are multiple, not the reality.
3
Reflect on closing remarks
Al-Jurjani ends by saying such doubts are many and one should be able to apply this method to others.

ثم قال الشارح:
قوله : ( أعني اشتباه العارض بالمعروض) الأنسب لما سبق أن يقول أعني اشتباه مفهوم الموضوع بما صدق عليه فكان الباعث على ما ذكره كون الشبه الموردة من اشتباه العارض بالمعروض.
قوله : (ذا قلب شيحان الشيحان بفتح الياء الغيور الحذر على حرمه استعارة للقلب الذي يدفع شين الشبه وعار الأغاليط عن بنات فكره .

The First Observation - Second Aim: On that His Essence is different from all other essences

This fallacy, I mean the confusion of the accident with the subject of attribution, is the source of many doubts in numerous places. So if you become aware of it—i.e., this origin—and know its state, and you have a vigilant heart (i.e., alert and protective of its sanctities which are the daughters of its thought), those doubts become clear to you, and you are able to refute others and are safe from being refuted by them. Among them is their saying: existence is common, for we are certain of it and hesitate about the particulars. So we say: That which is certain is the concept of existence, not the referent of existence, because it is possible that that concept is external to the realities of its differing individuals; thus the reality of existence is not a single common certain thing. The dispute is only about it, not about the concept that is accidental to its reality. Among them is their saying: existence is additional, for we conceive existence without essence, as in the Necessary example, and vice versa (i.e., we conceive essence without existence, as in the triangle), so existence is neither identical nor intrinsic. We said about it what preceded: the additional is its concept, not its reality. Among them is: unity is non-existential, otherwise there would be an infinite regress. We said: The necessary consequence of your proof, on the assumption of its validity, is that the concept of unity is non-existential, not existential, because then an infinite regress of existential units would follow. But this regress does not follow regarding the referent, for it is diverse: some is existential, some non-existential, some additional, some identical to the essence, as was indicated in the investigations of unity. Among them is: the attributes are additional to the essence, otherwise the concept of knowledge, power, and the other attributes would be one thing identical to the essence, and there is no doubt of its impossibility. We said: The referent (i.e., that which knowledge and power are true of) is one, but the concept is not one; rather, each has its own concept. And the like is more than can be enumerated, so let us suffice with what we have mentioned, since its state is not hidden from you.

Then the commentator said:
His statement: (I mean the confusion of the accident with the subject of attribution) – it would be more appropriate for what preceded to say: I mean the confusion of the concept of the subject with its referent, for the reason for what he mentioned is that the doubts cited arise from the confusion of the accident with the subject.
His statement: (vigilant heart) – “al-shiḥān” with a fatḥa on the yā’ means the one who is protective and cautious over his sanctities; it is a metaphor for the heart that repels the shame of doubt and the disgrace of fallacies from the daughters of its thought.
His statement: “We said about it what preceded” – If you say: The gist of the argument is that he says: one conceives the essence of existence even regarding the Necessary, so it would be additional to Him, and then the response would not be directed. I say: What is known in the supposed case is the neglect of conceiving the reality of the Necessary in its essence, and of conceiving it insofar as it is necessary in a certain manner, along with neglect of conceiving it in any manner.
His statement: (on the assumption of its validity) – An indication of the rebuttal by the possibility that the unity of unity is itself, and that the possibility of the existence of a nature does not necessitate the existence of all its individuals; so it is possible that unity is existential while the unity of unity is non-existential, as was similarly mentioned repeatedly.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of multiple doubts that arise from the fallacy of confusing the accident (ʿāriḍ) with the subject of attribution (maʿrūḍ). The commentator (al-Jurjani) begins by identifying this fallacy as the source of many doubts. He explains that if one understands this origin, one can refute these doubts and avoid being refuted. He then lists four specific doubts and answers them. The first doubt is that existence is common (mushtarak) because we are certain of its concept but hesitate in applying it to particulars. The answer: the certainty pertains to the concept (mafhūm) of existence, not its referent (mā ṣadaqa ʿalayhi). The concept may be external to the diverse realities of individuals, so the reality of existence is not a single common thing. The dispute is about the reality, not the concept. The second doubt: existence is additional (zāʾid) to essence because we can conceive existence without essence (e.g., the Necessary) and essence without existence (e.g., a triangle). The answer: what is additional is the concept, not the reality. The third doubt: unity is non-existential (ʿadamiyya), otherwise an infinite regress of units would follow. The answer: the regress would apply only to the concept of unity, not the referent, because the referent of unity is diverse—some existential, some non-existential, some additional, some identical to essence. The fourth doubt: the attributes are additional to the essence, otherwise the concepts of knowledge, power, etc. would be identical. The answer: the referent (i.e., what the attributes are true of) is one, but each attribute has a distinct concept. The commentator then explains each part of the matn: clarifying that the confusion is between concept and referent, explaining the metaphor of the vigilant heart, addressing a potential objection about the argument for the additional nature of existence, and noting that the rebuttal to the unity argument includes the possibility that the unity of unity itself may be non-existential.

صفات زائدة
مفهوم
ما صدق عليه

Key Takeaway
Attributes are distinct in concept but identical in referent to the essence.

The Fourth Argument: Division of Existence and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents and refutes the argument from division of existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the refutation of the fourth argument clarifies that being self-subsisting does not imply being a physical body.

Core Idea
Self-subsistence does not entail spatiality.

This passage introduces a common objection from those who argue that God must be a body because He is self-subsisting. The refutation hinges on rejecting a false equivalence between metaphysical independence and physical occupancy. Let's examine the argument and its answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the argument is structured as a division followed by a definition and conclusion.
The argument uses the terms qā'im bi nafsih and qā'im bi ghayrih.
2
Identify the two definitions being rejected
The refutation (man' al-tafsīrayn) rejects the equivalence of 'self-subsisting' with 'occupying space intrinsically' and 'subsisting by another' with 'occupying space derivatively'.
Sanusi provides alternative definitions in his answer.
3
Understand the logical move
Man' al-tafsīrayn is a method of refutation where the opponent's definitions are denied. Here, it shows that the conclusion does not follow because the definitions are not necessary.
This protects divine transcendence without denying God's self-subsistence.
4
Reflect on why this matters
Consider why theologians insist that God is not a body. This argument is one of many that must be refuted to maintain tanzīh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع الموجود ينقسم إلى قائم بنفسه وقائم بغيره والقائم بنفسه هو المتحيز بالذات والقائم بغيره هو المتحيز تبعاً هو ( أي الواجب تعالى ) قائم بنفسه فيكون متحيزا بذاته والجواب منع التفسيرين ( فإن القائم بنفسه هو المستغنى عن محل يقومه وليس يلزم من هذا كونه متحيز بذاته والقائم بغيره هو المحتاج إلى ذلك المحل ولا يلزم منه كونه متحيزاً تبعاً.
The fourth (argument): Existent things are divided into that which is self-subsisting and that which subsists by another. The self-subsisting is that which is intrinsically occupying space (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat), and that which subsists by another is that which is derivatively occupying space. He (i.e., the Necessary Existent, exalted is He) is self-subsisting, so He would be intrinsically occupying space. The answer: The two definitions (tafsīrān) are rejected. For the self-subsisting is that which is independent of a substrate that sustains it, and it does not follow from this that it is intrinsically occupying space; and that which subsists by another is that which is in need of that substrate, and it does not follow from this that it is derivatively occupying space.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument for God's corporeality and its refutation. The argument claims that all existents are either self-subsisting (qā'im bi nafsih) or subsisting by another (qā'im bi ghayrih). The self-subsisting is defined as 'that which occupies space intrinsically' (al-mutahayyiz bi al-dhāt), and the other as 'that which occupies space derivatively'. God is self-subsisting, so He must occupy space. The answer is a rejection (man') of both definitions: self-subsisting means independence from a sustaining substrate, not necessarily occupying space; subsisting by another means dependence on such a substrate, not necessarily derivatively occupying space. Thus the conclusion does not follow.

Key Terms
قائم بنفسه
قائم بغيره
متحيز بالذات
متحيز تبعا
منع التفسيرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'self-subsisting' necessarily implies 'occupying space' because in the physical world, independent substances are spatial.
Correction: The refutation clarifies that self-subsistence is a metaphysical property meaning independence from a substrate, and it does not imply spatial occupancy.

Checkpoint

B) God is not self-subsisting.
C) The division of existents is false.
D) The argument is based on scripture.

Key Takeaway
The refutation of the fourth argument shows that 'self-subsisting' does not imply 'occupying space', preserving God's transcendence.

?
If self-subsistence means independence from a substrate, not spatiality, then your mental picture of Allah as 'somewhere' is an idol. Will you commit today to catching and correcting that thought every time it appears?

First Proof: Temporal Event in God Would Be Eternal
First proof that a temporal event subsisting in God would be eternal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that a temporal event cannot subsist in God because it would entail an absurd change in essential modality.

Core Idea
Receptivity to a temporal event must be an eternal concomitant of God's essence.

This passage presents the first of three proofs against the possibility of temporal events subsisting in God. The argument uses the concept of receptivity (qabiliyya) and shows that if it were accidental, it would lead to contradiction. You will see how the author builds a conditional argument and why he stops short of addressing the second half.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of the proof
Notice that the author states the conclusion first: if a temporal event could subsist in God, then it would be eternal (which is false). Then he argues for the entailment.
The proof is a conditional with a false consequent.
2
Understand 'qabiliyya' (receptivity)
Receptivity is the attribute that allows an essence to receive an accident. The author claims it must be a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the essence itself.
If it were accidental, the essence would change its essential modality.
3
Follow the logical steps of the entailment
If receptivity is not a concomitant but accidental, then before it arises the essence is essentially impossible to accept the event, and after it arises it becomes essentially possible—an absurd change.
The same would apply if receptivity were removed later, but the author omits it.
4
Reflect on why the author stops
The author notes that he does not need to elaborate the case of removal because the first contradiction is sufficient to refute the premise.
This shows the economy of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا في إثبات هذا المدعي وجوه ثلاثة. الأول : لو جاز قيام الحادث بذاته لجاز أزلاً واللازم باطل أما الملازمة فلأن القابلية من لوازم الذات وإلا لزم الانقلاب من الامتناع الذاتي إلى الإمكان الذاتي فإن القابلية إذ لم تكن لازمة بل عارضة كان الذات في حد نفسها قبل عروض القابلية لها ممتنعة القبول للحادث المقبول وبعد عروضها ممكنة القبول له فيلزم ذلك الانقلاب ولو فرض زوال القابلية بعد ثبوتها لزم الانقلاب من الإمكان الذاتي إلى الامتناع الذاتي ولما لم يكن لنا حاجة إلى هذا لم نتعرض له
We have three proofs to establish this claim. The first: If it were permissible for a temporal event to subsist in His essence, then it would be permissible for it to be eternal. The consequent is false. As for the entailment, it is because the receptivity (al-qabiliyya) is a concomitant of the essence; otherwise a change from essential impossibility to essential possibility would follow. For if the receptivity is not a concomitant but rather accidental, the essence in itself before the occurrence of the receptivity would be impossible of accepting the temporal event, and after its occurrence possible of accepting it, so that change would follow. And if one supposed the removal of the receptivity after its subsistence, a change from essential possibility to essential impossibility would follow. And since we have no need for this, we did not address it.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof against the possibility of temporal events subsisting in God. The argument is structured as a conditional: if a temporal event could subsist in God, then it could be eternal (i.e., exist from eternity). The conclusion is false because temporal events are by definition contingent and cannot be eternal. The key premise is that the receptivity (al-qabiliyya) of God's essence to accept a temporal event must be an eternal concomitant (lazim) of the essence. If it were not a necessary concomitant but rather an accidental attribute, then before the receptivity occurred, God's essence would be essentially impossible (mumtani' dhati) for accepting that event, and after the receptivity occurred, it would become essentially possible (mumkin dhati). This entails a change from essential impossibility to essential possibility, which is absurd because essential attributes are immutable. Furthermore, if the receptivity could later cease, that would cause a change from essential possibility back to essential impossibility, also absurd. The author notes that they do not need to elaborate further on this point.

Key Terms
القابلية
اللازم
الامتناع الذاتي
الإمكان الذاتي
الانقلاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument simply assumes temporal events cannot be in God from the start, without logical reasoning.
Correction: The argument does not assume that; it shows that if such events were in God, they would have to be eternal, which is a contradiction. Moreover, the modal change argument provides a deeper absurdity.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what would follow if the receptivity (qabiliyya) in God's essence were accidental rather than a necessary concomitant?
A) The temporal event would be eternal.
B) The essence would change from essential impossibility to essential possibility.
C) The receptivity would be impossible.
D) The temporal event would be impossible.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that receptivity to a temporal event must be an eternal attribute of God, or else His essence would change from impossible to possible, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that God's essence cannot change in its fundamental receptivity. When you pray, do you ever assume that God's attitude toward you changes based on your deeds alone, as if His essential mercy or justice fluctuates? How does this truth about God's immutability affect your expectation of His responses to your repentance? Commit today to trusting that His attributes are fixed and that He does not change like humans do.
?
If God's essence could change, His promises would be unreliable. Is there an area of your life where you doubt God's promise because you feel unworthy or because your circumstances have changed? This lesson underscores that God's essence is eternally receptive to what is consistent with His nature. Which promise of God in the Qur'an do you need to hold onto right now, knowing that His essential attributes never waver? Write it down and repeat it as a personal declaration.
?
The argument warns against assuming that God can be receptive to temporal events that contradict His eternal nature. Think of a desire or plan you have that might be out of alignment with God's eternal commands. Are you trying to force God to accept a 'temporal event' in your life that goes against His immutable will? Repent of any attempt to change God's standard to fit your wishes, and submit to His eternal decree.
