The Argument from Accident and Its Opposite
Sanusi presents a four-premise argument against divine accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument against the possibility of accidents in God, which is based on the principle that an accident cannot be separated from its opposite, and will be able to identify its four premises.

Core Idea
An accident's opposite is also an accident, so God cannot have either.

This lesson presents a sophisticated argument that relies on the logical relationship between an accident and its opposite. The argument aims to show that if God possessed any accidental attribute, He would also possess its opposite, and thus would be subject to contingency. Sanusi outlines four premises that form the backbone of this reasoning, though he does not fully refute them in this passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style and the list of premises.
2
Identify the four premises
The passage enumerates four premises. Write them down in your own words. They form a chain leading to the conclusion.
The first and third premises are highlighted as problematic in the original text.
3
Understand the logical chain
Trace how the premises lead to the conclusion: if God had an accident, He would never be free of it or its opposite, hence He would be contingent.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Note the truncation
The passage ends before the refutation. Think about what might follow – how could one object to these premises?
The refutation is covered in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يقال لو قام الحادث بذاته لم يخل عنه وعن ضده وضد الحادث حادث وما لا يخلو الحادث فهو حادث (وهذا الاستدلال يبتني على أربع مقدمات. الأولى أن لكل صفة حادثة ضدا الثانية ضد الحادث حادث الثالثة الذات لا تخلو عن الشيء وضده الرابعة ما لا يخلو عن الحادث فهو حادث والثالث الأول ) من هذه المقدمات
And perhaps it is said: if an accident were to subsist in His essence, He would not be devoid of it and of its opposite, and the opposite of an accident is an accident, and whatever is not devoid of an accident is itself an accident. And this argument is based on four premises: first, that every accidental attribute has an opposite; second, that the opposite of an accident is an accident; third, that the essence is not devoid of a thing and its opposite; fourth, that whatever is not devoid of an accident is itself an accident. And the first and third of these premises...
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents an additional argument against the possibility of accidents in God: if God had an accident, He would never be free of it or its opposite (since the accident's opposite is also an accident), and whatever is never free from an accident is itself an accident—thus God would be contingent. The argument is built on four premises, which are listed. However, the text cuts off after mentioning that the first and third premises are... (the refutation would follow). This lesson only contains the statement of the argument.

Key Terms
ضد
الحادث
لا يخلو
المقدمات الأربع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God possesses one accident, He must also possess its opposite at the same time, making Him contradictory.
Correction: The argument does not claim simultaneous opposites; rather, it claims that over time God would be subject to change (alternating between an accident and its opposite), which implies temporality. The premises themselves are disputable, and the argument does not force contradiction.

Checkpoint
How many premises does the argument cited begin with?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Two

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument attempts to prove God's transcendence by showing that accidents imply opposites, but its premises are subject to dispute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage presents a logical argument but cuts off before its refutation. Are you comfortable basing your beliefs on arguments that are not fully resolved? What would it take for you to investigate the counterarguments to this position?
?
The premises of this argument are disputed. Think of a deeply held belief you have about God. What are the foundational premises that belief rests on? Have you ever critically examined them, or do you accept them simply because they are common?
?
If you were to encounter a theological argument that challenged your understanding, like this one, would you engage with it or dismiss it? What does your reaction reveal about your intellectual humility and trust in the scholarly tradition?

The Infinite Accident Contradiction and Its Resolution
Al-Tusi's contradiction argument and commentator's conditional resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical contradiction that al-Tusi identifies in the infinite succession of accidents on an eternal being, and the conditions under which that contradiction can be resolved.

Core Idea
Infinite accidents on an eternal being are not always contradictory—it depends on how priority is defined.

This passage presents a powerful argument from al-Tusi that seems to show an infinite chain of accidents on an eternal being is logically impossible. Yet the commentator offers a subtle resolution, showing that the contradiction only arises under specific assumptions. Let's explore both sides of this deep theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once, noting the flow from preliminary remarks to al-Tusi's argument and then the commentator's response.
2
Understand al-Tusi's core argument
Identify the key premises: the eternal must be prior to every accident; infinite accidents would prevent that priority; hence contradiction. Notice how he defines 'eternal' and 'accident'.
3
Examine the commentator's two conditions
The commentator says the contradiction only follows if (a) each accident's occurrence implies the whole collective sum, or (b) the eternal must be temporally prior to each accident. Understand why these conditions matter.
Consider how the commentator's view allows for priority in multiple times rather than a single prior state.
4
Reflect and test your understanding
Use the checkpoint to verify you can identify when the contradiction arises. Then do the activity to summarize the argument in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (الثالث منها أنه تعالى إلخ) فإن قلت هذا الوجه أيضاً منقوض بالمتجدد قلت ممنوع فإن التأثر عن غير الموجود اعتباري محض. قوله : ( وهذا ليس بلازم لأن أزلية الإمكان إلخ ) قد سبق في الأمور العامة اعتراض الشارح هذا الكلام وبيان الاستلزام بلا محذور فلذا لم يتعرض له هاهنا قوله : ( كما أشرنا إليه أي بقول موصوف في الأزل بصحة إيجاد العالم . قوله : ( ولا يلزم الخلو عن الكمال المشترك إلخ) يعني الكمال قديم محفوظ وجوده بتعاقب الأفراد الغير المتناهية، وحدوث كل فرد لا يستلزم حدوث ذلك النوع وإن لم يوجد إلا في ضمن فرد كما زعمه شارح المقاصد وهاهنا بحث ذكره الأستاذ المحقق الطوسي في الزخر وادعى فيه المتانة جداً، وهو أن القول بتوارد حوادث غير متناهية على قديم كلام متناقض لأن القديم يجب أن يكون سابقاً على كل حادث إذ المراد بالقديم ما لا يكون مسبوقاً بالعدم، وبالحادث ما يكون مسبوقاً به فلا بد أن يكون سابقاً على كل واحد مما يصدق عليه الحادث وهذا يوجب أن يكون له حالة يتحقق فيها سبقه على كل واحد مما يصدق عليه الحادث بضرورة المنافاة العقل ويلزم من توارد الحوادث الغير المتناهية عليه أن لا يوجد له تلك الحالة بل مقارنته دائماً مع بعض الحوادث وعدم خلوه عنه في حال من الأحوال، فلا يكون سابقاً على كل فرد منها إذ بين المقارنة مع بعض الأفراد والسبق على فرد بديهية انتهى كلامه، والحق أن التناقض إنما يلزم إذ استلزم حدوث كل فرد حدوث الكل المجموعي الذي هو عين مجموع الأفراد الموجودة، أو لزم سبق القديم على كل ما يصدق عليه الحادث في زمان إذ لو لم يستلزم وكفى السبق في أزمنة متعددة كما هو الظاهر لم يلزم ذلك كما لا يخفى على المصنف
His statement: 'The third: from them that He, exalted...' For if you say: this face also is refuted by the renewing, I say: it is denied, for the affect by a non-existent is purely conceptual. His statement: 'And this is not necessary because the eternity of possibility...' has already preceded in the general matters the objection of the commentator to this speech and the demonstration of entailment without harm, so he did not address it here. His statement: 'as we indicated,' i.e., by the statement described in eternity as the validity of originating the world. His statement: 'And the lack of the common perfection does not follow' means that perfection is eternal, its existence preserved by the succession of infinite individuals, and the occurrence of each individual does not entail the occurrence of that type, even if it exists only within an individual, as the commentator of al-Maqasid claimed. And here is a research mentioned by the master investigator al-Tusi in al-Zakhr, and he claimed its solidity greatly: namely, that the statement of the succession of infinite accidents on an eternal being is contradictory, because the eternal must be prior to every accident, since what is meant by eternal is what is not preceded by non-existence, and by accident what is preceded by it. So it must be prior to every instance that is described as an accident. This requires that it have a state in which its priority over every instance described as an accident is realized by necessity of rational contradiction. And the succession of infinite accidents upon it necessitates that it does not have that state, but rather it is always concomitant with some accidents and never devoid of them in any state, so it is not prior to every individual of them, for the incompatibility between concomitance with some individuals and priority over an individual is intuitive. End of his speech. And the truth is that the contradiction only follows if the occurrence of each individual entails the occurrence of the whole collective which is the sum of the existing individuals, or if the priority of the eternal over everything described as an accident is necessary in time. For if it does not entail and the priority in multiple times suffices, as is apparent, it does not follow, as is not hidden from the author.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the earlier arguments, clarifying and critiquing them. The commentator first addresses a potential objection to the third argument, then explains a previous point about entailment. He then discusses the concept of a common perfection preserved through infinite individuals. A key part is a citation from al-Tusi arguing that the idea of infinite accidents succeeding on an eternal being is contradictory, because the eternal must be prior to every accident, but infinite succession would prevent that priority. The commentator then gives his own view: the contradiction only follows if the occurrence of each individual entails the occurrence of the whole collective or if priority must be temporal; otherwise, priority in multiple times suffices and no contradiction arises.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite succession on an eternal being is always logically impossible.
Correction: It is only contradictory if the succession implies the existence of a collective whole or if the eternal must be temporally prior to every individual accident.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, when does the infinite succession of accidents on an eternal being become contradictory?
A) Always
B) Only if it implies the occurrence of the collective whole or temporal priority of the eternal
C) Only if the accidents are simultaneous
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Al-Tusi's contradiction is not absolute; it depends on whether the eternal must be temporally prior to every accident or whether the accidents collectively form a whole.

?
Al-Tusi presents a contradiction that seems logically airtight, yet the commentator offers a resolution. Have you ever clung to a seemingly perfect argument about God, only to realize it rested on a hidden assumption? What would it take for you to hold your theological convictions with both intellectual rigor and openness to deeper insight? Pause and identify one such assumption in your own thinking.
?
The commentator says priority 'in multiple times suffices.' Does your worship reflect a God who is engaged with every moment of your life, not just as a distant first cause but as one who is present with you in time? If not, what changes in your daily routine would realign your awareness of His constant priority over your temporal existence? Name one specific action you will take within the next 48 hours.

The Sufficiency of Possible Prevention
Response to an objection on the condition for impotence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that impotence in the dualist argument is entailed by the mere possibility of prevention, regardless of whether the evil acts by choice or necessity.

Core Idea
Potential prevention alone suffices for impotence, even if evil is necessitated.

This passage addresses a subtle objection against the dualist argument: if the evil being acts by necessity rather than choice, does inability to prevent still imply impotence? The commentator clarifies that the possibility of prevention in itself is enough, removing any ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: it claims impotence only follows if the evil being acts by choice, not by necessity. Why would choice matter? Consider the dualist description of light as living, knowing, powerful.
The objection assumes that only voluntary actions can be subject to prevention.
2
Examine the commentator's response
The commentator rejects the restriction: the possibility of prevention in itself (إمكان الاندفاع) is sufficient. Even if the evil is necessitated, the good being's inability to prevent it indicates impotence.
Necessity from another (وجوب بالغير) does not negate the possibility of prevention.
3
Connect to prior knowledge
Recall from earlier that the dualist argument hinges on the good being's ability to prevent evil. This passage reaffirms that the standard is simply whether prevention is possible, not whether the evil is freely chosen.
4
Reflect on the logical force
Consider why this rebuttal is decisive: if the good being could prevent an evil but does not, that inability is impotence regardless of the evil's cause. This preserves the argument against dualism.
The objection would have weakened the argument, but the commentator restores it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإن لم يقدر عليه فهو عاجز قيل إنما يلزم العجز إذا استند الشرور إلى الشرير بالاختيار كما يشير إليه قولهم في النور أنه حي عالم ،قادر، وأما إذا استندت إليه بالإيجاب فلا وقد عرفت مما سبق أن إمكان الاندفاع في نفس الأمر يكفي في لزوم العجز ولا ينافيه الوجوب بالغير.
His saying: 'And if he is not able to prevent it, then he is impotent.' It is said: Impotence is only entailed if the evils are attributed to the evil one by choice, as indicated by their statement about light that it is living, knowing, powerful. But if they are attributed to him by necessity, then it is not entailed. And you have known from what preceded that the possibility of prevention in itself is sufficient for the entailment of impotence, and it is not contradicted by necessity from another.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection (قيل) against the earlier conditional argument: if the good being cannot prevent evil, then he is impotent. The objection argues that impotence (عجز) only follows if the evil being acts by choice (اختيار), because the dualists claim that the good being (light) is living, knowing, powerful, implying that the evil being also acts by choice. However, if the evil being produces evil by necessity (إيجاب), then inability to prevent does not imply impotence. The commentator counters by stating that the mere possibility of prevention in itself (إمكان الاندفاع في نفس الأمر) is sufficient to entail impotence, regardless of whether the evil is necessitated. This necessity does not negate the fact that the good being could have prevented it but did not, so impotence is still entailed.

Key Terms
عجز
اختيار
إيجاب
إمكان الاندفاع
وجوب بالغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Impotence only applies if the evil being acts freely by choice.
Correction: Impotence is entailed even if the evil act is necessitated, as long as prevention is possible in principle.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is sufficient for impotence to be entailed?
A) That the evil being acts by choice.
B) That the good being is unable to prevent evil, even if the evil is necessitated, as long as prevention is possible in principle.
C) That the good being does not know how to prevent evil.
D) That the evil being is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of prevention in itself, not the mode of the evil act, determines the entailment of impotence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see evil or injustice, do you convince yourself that it is 'necessitated' or 'beyond your control' to avoid the burden of action? How does this lesson expose that excuse as an admission of your own impotence—or your failure to act on the possibility you had?
?
Is there an area in your life where you have the possibility to prevent harm—to a relationship, to your own soul, or to others—but you have rationalized inaction? If possibility alone suffices for impotence, what does your inaction reveal about you?
?
The lesson shows that even if evil is produced by necessity, the failure to prevent it still entails impotence. Who are you blaming—circumstances, others, fate—for the evil you could have prevented? Will you now own that possibility and act?

The Proof That God's Power Encompasses All Possible Things
Establishes the proof for the generality of God's power over all possible things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for the generality of divine power based on the requirer (divine essence) and corrector (possibility) and the equality of the relation to all possible things.

Core Idea
The proof rests on the equal relation of God's essence to all possibles through possibility as the condition for being an object of power.

This lesson presents the core argument that God's power extends to every possible thing. The proof identifies two principles: the requirer of power is the divine essence, and the corrector that makes something an object of power is its possibility. Since the relation of the essence is equal to all possibles, if power applies to one, it applies to all.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two key terms: muqtaḍā and muṣaḥḥiḥ
The passage introduces these two concepts. The muqtaḍā (requirer) of power is the divine essence; the muṣaḥḥiḥ (corrector) for being an object of power is possibility (imkān). Understand each.
These are foundational for the proof.
3
Understand why self-necessity and self-impossibility prevent being an object of power
The text says essential necessity (wujūb dhātī) and essential impossibility (imtināʿ dhātī) render something incapable of being an object of power. Grasp that only possibles can be objects of power.
4
Grasp the conclusion from equality of relation
Since the divine essence relates equally to all possible things, if power is proven over some, it is proven over all. This is the logical conclusion.
This step ties the proof together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن مخالفتها للقدرة الحادثة ليست أشد من مخالفة بعضها لبعض فلا يلزم عدم صلاحيتها لما ذكر. البحث الثاني: في أن قدرته تعالى تعم سائر الممكنات أي جميعها. والدليل عليه أن المقتضى للقدرة هو الذات لوجوب استناد صفاته إلى ذاته، والمصحح للمقدورية هو الإمكان لأن الوجوب والامتناع الذاتيين يحيلان المقدورية، ونسبة الذات إلى جميع الممكنات على السوية، وإذا أثبتت قدرته على بعضها تثبت على كلها. وهذا الاستدلال بناء على ما ذهب إليه أهل الحق من أن المعدوم ليس بشيء وإنما هو نفي محض ولا امتياز فيه أصلاً ولا تخصيص قطعاً فلا يتصور اختلاف في نسبة الذات إلى المعدومات بوجه من الوجوه، خلافاً للمعتزلة و من أن المعدوم لا مادة له ولا صورة خلافاً للحكماء وإلا لم يمتنع اختصاص البعض بمقدوريته تعالى دون بعض كما يقوله الخصم، فعلى قاعدة الاعتزال جاز أن تكون خصوصية بعض المعدومات الثابتة المتميزة مانعة من تعلق القدرة به وعلى قانون الحكمة جاز أن تستعد المادة لحدوث ممكن دون آخر، وعلى التقديرين لا تكون نسبة الذات إلى جميع الممكنات على سواء.
That its opposition to created power is not more severe than the opposition of some of them to others, so the lack of validity for what was mentioned does not follow. The second investigation: concerning that His power, exalted is He, encompasses all possible things, i.e., all of them. The proof for it is that the requirer of power is the essence due to the necessity of attributing His attributes to His essence, and the corrector for being an object of power is possibility, because essential necessity and essential impossibility prevent being an object of power, and the relation of the essence to all possible things is equal, and if His power is proven over some of them, it is proven over all of them. And this inference is based on what the people of truth have gone to, that the non-existent is not a thing, but rather it is pure negation, and there is no distinction in it at all, and no specification absolutely, so it is inconceivable that there be a difference in the relation of the essence to non-existents in any way, contrary to the Mu'tazila and those who hold that the non-existent has neither matter nor form, contrary to the philosophers; otherwise, the specification of some to be within His power rather than others, as the opponent claims, would not be prevented. For on the Mu'tazilī principle, it is possible that the specificity of some non-existents, which are fixed and distinguished, prevents the attachment of power to it, and on the law of philosophy, it is possible that matter be prepared for the occurrence of one possible and not another, and on both estimates, the relation of the essence to all possible things is not equal.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents the proof that God's power encompasses all possible things. It begins by stating that the proof relies on two elements: the requirer (muqtaḍā) of power is the divine essence, and the corrector (muṣaḥḥiḥ) for something being an object of power is its possibility (imkān). This is because self-necessity (wujūb dhātī) and self-impossibility (imtināʿ dhātī) render something incapable of being an object of power. Since the essence's relation to all possibles is equal, if power is proven over some, it is proven over all. The passage then explains that this inference is built on the Ash'arī doctrine that the non-existent (ma'dūm) is not a thing but pure negation with no inherent distinctions or specificities. Thus, no variation is possible in the essence's relation to non-existents. This opposes the Mu'tazila, who consider non-existents as fixed entities, and the philosophers, who give non-existents matter and form. On those views, some non-existents could be excluded from divine power due to specificities or material preparedness, which would break the equality of the essence's relation.

Key Terms
قدرة
ممكن
مقتضى
مصحح
إمكان
وجوب ذاتي
امتناع ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the corrector (muṣaḥḥiḥ) for something being an object of divine power according to this passage?
A) The divine essence itself
B) Possibility (imkān)
C) Essential necessity
D) Created power

Key Takeaway
God's power extends to all possible things because the divine essence relates equally to all possibles, with possibility being the sole condition for being an object of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power extends to all possible things, what does that mean for the things you consider impossible in your life? Are you limiting God's power by your own definitions of possibility? How can you realign your expectations with the truth that nothing possible is beyond His reach?
?
The proof states that the divine essence relates equally to all possibles. Yet you often feel that God's power is more present in some situations than others. Where in your life have you unconsciously denied His equal relation to all things? What step will you take today to trust His power even in a situation you've considered hopeless?
?
You say you believe God's power encompasses all possible things, but do you truly live as if that includes your personal struggles, relationships, and future? What specific area of your life have you been treating as if God's power does not extend there?

Why the Non-Existent Must Be Nothing: The Foundation of the Proof
Explains the Ash'ari doctrine of non-existents and its role in the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari doctrine of the non-existent as pure negation is necessary for the proof of universal divine power, and how the Mu'tazila and philosophers undermine it.

Core Idea
The proof's equality relies on non-existents having no distinctions; Ash'aris affirm this, while Mu'tazila and philosophers disagree.

This lesson examines the underpinning of the previous proof: the ontological status of the non-existent (al-ma'dūm). The Ash'ari position that non-existents are pure negation with no inherent distinctions ensures that the divine essence relates equally to all non-existents. The Mu'tazila consider non-existents fixed entities, and the philosophers give them matter and form, which allows for differences that could restrict divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. This passage explains the theoretical foundation of the previous proof.
2
Understand the Ash'ari view of non-existents
The text says: 'the non-existent is not a thing, but pure negation, with no distinction or specification.' This is the key point.
3
See how the Mu'tazila and philosophers differ
The Mu'tazila treat non-existents as fixed entities with specificities; the philosophers give non-existents matter and form. Both introduce distinctions.
4
Realize why these alternative views break the equality
If non-existents have distinctions, the relation of the essence is not equal, so the proof from equal relation fails. The opponent could then claim some possibles are excluded from divine power.
This shows the necessity of the Ash'ari position for the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents the proof that God's power encompasses all possible things. It begins by stating that the proof relies on two elements: the requirer (muqtaḍā) of power is the divine essence, and the corrector (muṣaḥḥiḥ) for something being an object of power is its possibility (imkān). This is because self-necessity (wujūb dhātī) and self-impossibility (imtināʿ dhātī) render something incapable of being an object of power. Since the essence's relation to all possibles is equal, if power is proven over some, it is proven over all. The passage then explains that this inference is built on the Ash'arī doctrine that the non-existent (ma'dūm) is not a thing but pure negation with no inherent distinctions or specificities. Thus, no variation is possible in the essence's relation to non-existents. This opposes the Mu'tazila, who consider non-existents as fixed entities, and the philosophers, who give non-existents matter and form. On those views, some non-existents could be excluded from divine power due to specificities or material preparedness, which would break the equality of the essence's relation.

Key Terms
معدوم
أهل الحق
معتزلة
حكماء
نفي محض
امتياز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari view that non-existents are nothing denies God's knowledge of them.
Correction: The Ash'ari view is about ontological status, not knowledge. God knows non-existents as objects of knowledge even though they are not things.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position stated in this passage, what is the ontological status of the non-existent (al-ma'dūm)?
A) It is a fixed entity with distinct characteristics.
B) It is pure negation, not a thing, with no distinction or specification.
C) It is a possible entity that requires matter to exist.
D) It is a substance with both matter and form.

Key Takeaway
For the proof of universal divine power to hold, non-existents must have no inherent distinctions; the Ash'ari position alone ensures this equality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that nothing—no sin, no situation, no aspect of your future—is beyond God's absolute power? Or do you secretly think some things are 'too far gone'? This lesson demands you dismantle that lie and trust in His universal power. What is one area you will consciously surrender to His power today?
?
The Mu'tazila introduced 'specificities' in non-existents that could limit God's power. What 'specificities' do you unconsciously attach to your problems—like 'this is too hard' or 'I'm too broken'—that make you doubt God's ability to change them? Write them down and counter each with the truth that non-existents have no distinctions.
?
If your past sins are merely non-existent with no inherent reality, then God's power over forgiveness and transformation is limitless. Have you been holding onto guilt as if your sin has a permanent 'distinction'? Confess that to God and commit to seeing yourself as He does—a possible person fully within His power to purify.

First Rational Argument and Answer on Psychic Speech
Al-Jurjani presents and answers the argument that command in eternity would be futile.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational argument against eternal psychic speech and the Ash'ari response that distinguishes between verbal and psychic commands.

Core Idea
Eternal commands need no present audience — only verbal speech does.

The Mu'tazilites argued that an eternal command without a hearer would be irrational. Al-Jurjani presents their objection and then the Ash'ari defense: the foolishness applies only to verbal commands, not to the inner attribute of psychic speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the argument and answer are clearly separated.
2
Identify the two parts
The passage has two distinct sections: the first rational argument (objection) and the answer to it. Identify where each begins.
The objection ends with the question, the answer begins with 'والجواب'.
3
Understand the key distinction
The answer hinges on the difference between verbal speech (اللفظ) and psychic speech (الكلام النفسي). Why is only the first subject to foolishness?
Think of the analogy of intending to teach an unborn child.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المعقول فوجهان: الأول الأمر والخبر في الأزل ولا مأمور ولا سامع فيه سفه فكيف يتصور ثبوته لله سبحانه وتعالى. والجواب عن الأول أن ذلك السفه الذي ادعيتموه إنما هو في اللفظ وأما الكلام النفسي فلا سفه فيه كطلب التعلم من ابن سيولد
As for the rational [arguments], there are two aspects: First, command and report in eternity — with no one commanded and no hearer — would be foolish (safah), so how can its existence be conceived for God, exalted is He? And the answer to the first is that that foolishness which you have claimed is only in verbal [speech]; as for psychic speech, there is no foolishness in it, like seeking learning from an unborn child.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents the first rational argument of the Mu'tazilites against the Ash'ari doctrine of psychic speech, followed by the Ash'ari answer. The argument runs: if God's speech is eternal, then in the state of pre-eternity (al-azal) there were no beings to command and no one to hear the report; issuing a command without anyone to obey or a report without anyone to hear is futile (safah), which is unworthy of God. Therefore, eternal speech cannot be attributed to God. The Ash'ari answer distinguishes between verbal speech (al-lafz) and psychic speech (al-kalam al-nafsi). The foolishness of commanding without a subject only applies to external verbal commands. Psychic speech is an internal attribute of intention and meaning; just as one can have the inner intention to teach a future child before the child exists, so too can God have eternal psychic commands and reports without there being present hearers or addressees. Thus the objection does not refute psychic speech.

Key Terms
الأمر
الخبر
الأزل
السفه
الكلام النفسي
اللفظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any command without a recipient is inherently absurd.
Correction: Only verbal commands require a recipient; psychic commands (inner intention) do not, as shown by intending to teach an unborn child.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari answer, why is an eternal command not foolish?
A) Because God can command non-existent beings directly
B) Because the command is psychic, not verbal, and does not require a present recipient
C) Because the command is only for future beings who will exist
D) Because foolishness does not apply to God at all

Key Takeaway
Eternal psychic speech is not foolish because it is an inner attribute of meaning, not a verbal command requiring a present audience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat God's eternal commands as mere suggestions that only apply when you feel ready to obey, ignoring that they exist as real, inner demands on your soul regardless of your circumstances? What would it mean for you to submit to a command that was issued before you were born?
?
The analogy of a teacher intending to teach an unborn child shows that intention precedes audience. In your own life, do you wait for perfect conditions before forming good intentions, or do you hold firm intentions to obey and serve, even when results are not yet visible?
?
Since God's psychic speech is eternal and not dependent on your presence, your obedience is not for God's benefit but for your own. How does this reframe your accountability for actions done in private, when no one else sees? Will you live today as if you are always in the presence of an eternal command?

The Second Rational Argument on Eternal Speech and Its Answer
Al-Jurjani presents and answers the argument that eternal speech would relate to all objects equally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational argument against eternal speech and how al-Jurjani answers it by showing that an eternal attribute can relate selectively.

Core Idea
An eternal attribute does not have to relate equally to all objects.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents a powerful Mu'tazilite argument: if God's speech is eternal, it must relate to all possible objects equally, like knowledge. This would make every act both commanded and forbidden, a contradiction. But the answer shows that an eternal thing can be selective. Let's see how.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the mention of 'slip of the pen' in some manuscripts.
2
Identify the argument's structure
Break down the argument: premise (eternal speech -> equal relation), comparison with knowledge, implication for moral law, resulting contradiction.
3
Examine the answer
See how al-Jurjani responds: an eternal entity suitable for many things may relate to only some. This avoids the contradiction.
The answer is concise but powerful.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this answer is important for the doctrine of eternal divine speech. It shows that eternity does not force all-encompassing relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: لو كان كلامه تعالى قديما لاستوى نسبته إلى جميع المتعلقات لأنه حينئذ يكون كالعلم في أن تعلقه بمتعلقاته يكون لذاته، فكما أن علمه يتعلق بجميع ما يصح تعلقه به كذلك كلامه يتعلق بكل ما يصح تعلقه به ولما كان الحسن والقبح بالشرع صح في كل فعل أن يؤمر به وأن ينهي فيلزم تعلق أمره ونهيه بالأفعال كلها فيكون كل فعل مأمورا به ومنهيا عنه معا هذا خلف، وقد وقع في بعض النسخ كالعلم والقدرة وهو سهو من القلم. والجواب عن الثاني أن الشيء القديم الصالح للأمور المتعددة قد يتعلق ببعض من تلك الأمور
Second: If His speech were eternal, its relation to all objects of relation would be equal, because then it would be like knowledge in that its relation to its objects is by its essence. Just as His knowledge relates to everything that can be related to it, so too His speech would relate to everything that can be related to it. And since good and evil are according to divine law, it would be valid for every act to be commanded and forbidden, so His command and prohibition would be attached to all acts, and thus every act would be both commanded and forbidden together — this is a contradiction. And in some manuscripts [the phrase] 'like knowledge and power' occurs, but that is a slip of the pen. And the answer to the second is that an eternal thing suitable for multiple things may relate to some of those things.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents the second rational argument against eternal psychic speech, followed by its answer. The argument: if God's speech is eternal, it must relate equally to all possible objects, like divine knowledge which relates to all knowable things. Since moral values are determined by revelation (shar'), any act could be commanded or forbidden. If God's eternal speech related equally to all acts, then every act would be simultaneously commanded and forbidden — a logical contradiction. Therefore, speech cannot be eternal. The answer is simple: an eternal attribute that is suitable for many objects may still relate only to some of them selectively, just as divine power relates to some possibles at specific times. Hence eternal speech does not require relating to all acts equally, and the contradiction is avoided.

Key Terms
قديم
استواء النسبة
المتعلقات
الحسن والقبح بالشرع
الأمر والنهي
سهو القلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal attribute must relate to all objects equally.
Correction: An eternal thing can be suitable for many things but relate selectively to some, as al-Jurjani explains.

Checkpoint
Why does the Mu'tazilite argument claim that eternal speech would lead to a contradiction?
A) Because eternal speech would be finite
B) Because it would require all acts to be both commanded and forbidden simultaneously
C) Because God cannot have both command and prohibition
D) Because speech cannot be eternal

Key Takeaway
Eternal speech does not require relating to all acts equally, so the contradiction is avoided.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that because God's speech is eternal, it must be abstract and distant from your daily struggles? Yet the answer shows His speech relates specifically to some acts. How might this specific relation affect the way you approach a divine command you find difficult today? Will you take it as a personal address from the Eternal to you here and now, or still as a general rule?
?
When you hear a command in the Qur'an that seems to apply only in certain contexts, do you ever feel it is less divine because it is not 'universal' in a uniform sense? This lesson reminds you that selectivity does not diminish eternity. How does that challenge your expectation of what God's speech should be like? Will you now listen more carefully for the wisdom behind why God chose to command some things and not others, rather than demanding that all commands feel equally immediate?
?
The argument against eternal speech was that it would entail every act being both commanded and forbidden—a contradiction. The answer: an eternal thing can relate to some without relating to all. Is there a contradiction in your own life—something you simultaneously feel commanded and forbidden to do by your own standards? How does God's selective command here free you from that confusion? Will you submit to His specific command even if your reason wants to make it absolute?

The Mu'tazilite Two-Fold Proof Against Divine Lying
Presents the two Mu'tazilite proofs against divine lying: as ugly act and as harmful to welfare.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments the Mu'tazilites used to prove that lying is impossible for God.

Core Idea
Lying is impossible for God because it is ugly and harms the world.

The Mu'tazilites argued that God cannot lie based on two premises: one from the nature of lying as an ugly act, and another from the obligation to do what is most beneficial for creation. This lesson presents these two arguments as a foundation for understanding the debate on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite arguments
Read the Arabic passage containing the two reasons. Notice how each reason is presented as a logical inference.
The first reason relies on rational ethics; the second on the principle of al-aṣlaḥ.
2
Identify the two reasons
Distinguish the first reason (lying is ugly) from the second (lying harms welfare). Understand the basis of each.
The second reason is linked to the Mu'tazilite doctrine of wujūb al-aṣlaḥ.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the Mu'tazilites thought these reasons prove impossibility. How do they define 'speech' as action rather than attribute?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا كما توهموه ( تفريع على ( ثبوت ( الكلام ) لله تعالى وهو أنه ( يمتنع عليه الكذب اتفاقاً أما عند المعتزلة فلوجهين : الأول أنه أي الكذب في الكلام الذي هو عندهم من قبيل الأفعال دون الصفات ( قبيح وهو ( سبحانه ( لا يفعل القبيح وهو بناء على أصلهم في إثبات حكم العقل) بحسن الأفعال وقبحها مقيسة إلى الله تعالى وستعرف بطلانه . ( الثاني : أنه مناف لمصلحة العالم لأنه إذا جاز وقوع الكذب في كلامه ارتفع الوثوق عن إخباره بالثواب والعقاب وسائر ما أخبر به من أحوال الآخرة والأولى وفي ذلك فوات مصالح لا تحصى ( والأصلح واجب عليه ) تعالى عندهم يجوز إخلاله) به والجواب : منع وجوب الأصلح، إذ لا يجب عليه شيء أصلا بل هو عن ذلك قطعاً، قوله: (والجواب منع وجوب (الأصلح فإن قلت : سلمنا ذلك لكن الكذب في الكلام ينافي حكمة إرسال الرسل ويجاب بأن إحاطة كل حكمة مما لا يأتي في القوى القاصرة فلعل فيما فعله حكمة جليلة لا تطلع عليها .
He is exalted above what they imagine (a branching from the establishment of speech for God, and that is that lying is impossible for Him by consensus. As for the Mu'tazila, it is for two reasons: The first: that lying, in speech which is for them of the category of actions, not attributes, is ugly (qabīḥ), and He (exalted) does not do the ugly, and this is based on their principle in establishing the ruling of the intellect regarding the goodness and ugliness of actions, measured to God, and you will know its falsity. The second: that it is incompatible with the welfare of the world because if the occurrence of lying in His speech were permitted, trust in His informing of reward and punishment and all that He informed of the states of the Hereafter and the First would be lifted, and in that is the loss of countless benefits, and the most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) is obligatory upon Him (exalted) according to them, and it is not permissible for Him to violate it. The answer: prevention of the obligation of al-aṣlaḥ, since nothing is obligatory upon Him at all, rather He is absolutely free of that. His saying: (The answer is prevention of the obligation of al-aṣlaḥ). For if you say: we grant that, but lying in speech contradicts the wisdom of sending messengers. And the answer is that encompassment of every wisdom is not within weak powers; perhaps in what He does there is a profound wisdom that you do not perceive.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite proof against the possibility of lying in God's speech and the Ash'arite response. The author begins by stating that lying is impossible for God by consensus. He then gives the Mu'tazilite two-fold argument: first, lying is an ugly act (qabīḥ), and God does not do ugly acts, based on their principle of rational judgment of good and evil. Second, lying would destroy trust in God's reports about reward, punishment, and the afterlife, contradicting the obligation of the most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) which they hold is obligatory upon God. The Ash'arite response is a simple denial (man‘) of the obligation of al-aṣlaḥ, asserting that nothing is obligatory upon God. The passage then includes a commentary (indicated by 'qāla') that addresses an objection: even if wujūb al-aṣlaḥ is denied, lying still contradicts the wisdom of sending messengers. The response argues that not every wisdom is accessible to limited human intellect; there may be a profound wisdom in His actions that we do not perceive.

Key Terms
الكذب
القبح
الأصلح
وجوب الأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite first reason, why is lying impossible for God?
A) Because lying is an attribute of imperfection.
B) Because lying is ugly and God does not do ugly acts.
C) Because lying would contradict the obligation of al-aṣlaḥ.
D) Because lying is forbidden in revelation.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites argued that lying is impossible for God using both deontological and consequentialist reasoning based on their principles of rational ethics and wujūb al-aṣlaḥ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every word from God is utterly trustworthy and impossible to break, how would that change the way you approach reading the Qur'an today? What specific verse do you need to trust more deeply?
?
The Mu'tazilites argued that God cannot lie because it would undermine all trust in His promises. Is there any promise of God that you struggle to believe will actually come true for you? Why, and what would it mean to you if that promise failed?
?
Consider the second reason: if lying were possible, trust in divine reward and punishment would collapse. Are there areas in your life where you act as if God's promises of reward or warnings of punishment are not fully credible? What is one step you can take today to realign your actions with the certainty of God's word?

The Ash'arite Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Proof
Presents the Ash'arite response to the Mu'tazilite proof: denial of wujūb al-aṣlaḥ and the appeal to hidden wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite response denying the obligation of al-aṣlaḥ and how they defend divine wisdom.

Core Idea
God is not bound by any obligation; His actions are wise but not required to be the most beneficial.

The Ash'arites countered the Mu'tazilite proof by simply denying the premise that God is obligated to do the most beneficial (wujūb al-aṣlaḥ). They argue that nothing is obligatory upon God. This lesson explains that response and how they address the objection that lying contradicts the wisdom of sending messengers, pointing to the limitations of human understanding.

Guided Path
1
Examine the Ash'arite denial
Read the passage where the author provides the response: 'man‘ wujūb al-aṣlaḥ'. Understand that this is a direct rebuttal of the Mu'tazilite second reason.
The denial is based on the principle that God is sovereign and not subject to external obligations.
2
Analyze the objection and answer
The text raises an objection: even if no obligation, lying still undermines the purpose of sending messengers. The answer appeals to hidden wisdom beyond human comprehension.
This is a typical Ash'arite appeal to divine transcendence.
3
Reflect on the underlying theological principles
Consider how this debate reflects differing views on divine justice and human reason.
Mu'tazilites emphasize rational obligation; Ash'arites emphasize divine sovereignty.

?

Deficiency in Action and Rational Ugliness Are Identical
Refutation of the claim that deficiency in action differs from rational ugliness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that deficiency in action and rational ugliness are identical, and how this refutes the objection against the Ash'ari position.

Core Idea
Deficiency in action and rational ugliness are the same thing, expressed differently.

This passage addresses a key objection against the Ash'ari position on rational ugliness. The author clarifies that deficiency in action (which even Ash'aris accept) is identical to rational ugliness, thus undermining the objection that Ash'aris cannot use deficiency arguments. The commentator supports this by noting that al-Abhari, a student of the author, follows the same view.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice the key terms: النقص في الفعل, القبح العقلي.
2
Identify the objection being refuted
The objection is that denying rational ugliness contradicts using deficiency in action to reject lying. Find where the author addresses this.
Look for the rhetorical question: 'كيف يتمسكون...'
3
Grasp the author's claim of identity
The author states that deficiency in action is exactly rational ugliness itself; only the expression differs. Understand why this refutes the objection.
If they are identical, then using deficiency arguments is consistent with denying rational ugliness.
4
Consider the commentator's support
The commentator mentions al-Abhari's agreement. Note that this strengthens the author's point.
5
Connect to the broader argument
Reflect on how this passage fits into the larger discussion of divine attributes and rational judgments. The author defends the Ash'ari approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دفعه بقوله : ( واعلم أنه لم يظهر لي فرق بين النقص في الفعل وبين القبح العقلي ) فيه ) فإن النقص في الأفعال هو القبح العقلي بعينه ) فيها ( وإنما تختلف العبارة) دون المعنى، فأصحابنا المنكرون للقبح العقلي كيف يتمسكون في دفع الكذب عن الكلام اللفظي بلزوم النقص في أفعاله تعالى. الشارح تبع فيه الأبهري وهو تلميذ المصنف فاعلم بمراده وإلا فالأقرب أن نجعل اعتراضاً على أصل الدليل في المتن فإنه لما قيل : إن الكذب نقص وهو محال عليه تعالى وأفاد ظاهراً أن لا يكون كاذباً في كلامه على الإطلاق، اتجه أن يقال : إنما يدل ذلك على انتفاء الكذب عليه مطلقاً لو كان النقص في فعله غير القبح العقلي الذي نحن لانقول به لكن لم يظهر كونه غيره .
He (the author) rebuts it by saying: 'Know that it has not become clear to me a difference between deficiency in the action and rational ugliness.' For deficiency in actions is exactly rational ugliness itself; only the expression differs, not the meaning. How can our companions who deny rational ugliness cling to rejecting lying from verbal speech by invoking deficiency in His actions? The commentator follows al-Abhari in this, and he is a student of the author, so know his intent. Otherwise, it is more appropriate to make it an objection to the original proof in the text: for when it was said that lying is a deficiency and it is impossible for God, and it apparently implies that He cannot be a liar in His speech at all, it is directed to say: that only indicates the negation of lying from Him absolutely if deficiency in His action is other than rational ugliness which we do not affirm, but it is not clear that it is other.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is an objection and response regarding the relationship between deficiency in action and rational ugliness. The author (al-Iji) states that he sees no difference between deficiency in action and rational ugliness; they are the same thing expressed differently. The commentator then notes that al-Abhari (a student of al-Iji) follows this view. The point is that those who deny rational ugliness (the Ash'aris) still rely on the concept of deficiency in actions to reject lying in verbal speech. If deficiency and rational ugliness are identical, then this reliance is valid. If they are different, then the argument from deficiency would only work if deficiency is not rational ugliness, but it is not shown to be different. Thus, the objection that the Ash'aris cannot use the deficiency argument because they deny rational ugliness is refuted.

القبح العقلي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One cannot deny rational ugliness and still use deficiency in action as an argument, because deficiency in action implies rational ugliness.
Correction: The author shows that deficiency in action and rational ugliness are identical in meaning, so using deficiency arguments is consistent with denying rational ugliness as a separate category.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the relationship between deficiency in action (النقص في الفعل) and rational ugliness (القبح العقلي)?
A) They are completely different
B) They are identical in meaning, only the expression differs
C) Deficiency in action is a subset of rational ugliness
D) Rational ugliness is a type of deficiency in action

Key Takeaway
The author affirms that deficiency in action and rational ugliness are identical in meaning, resolving the apparent contradiction in the Ash'ari position.

The author says only the expression differs, not the meaning. How often do you hide behind different words to avoid admitting the true moral state of your actions? Give one concrete example you will change today.

The Objection That the Argument Also Proves Truth Impossible
Response to the objection that the argument proves truth impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the response to the objection relies on distinguishing between verbal and psychological speech, and why the objection fails for psychological speech.

Core Idea
The distinction between verbal and psychological speech saves the argument from proving truth impossible.

This lesson deals with an important objection to the argument that lying is impossible for God. The objector claims the same reasoning would make truth impossible. The response introduces a crucial distinction between outward verbal speech and inner psychological speech. We will see how this distinction answers the objection and why it is central to the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the claim that the argument would also prove truth impossible. Notice how the objector uses the same premise of eternity.
The objector says: if truth were eternal, lying would be impossible, but we know that one who knows something can inform truthfully.
2
Examine the response
The response denies the necessity claim by distinguishing between verbal and psychological speech. Verbal speech can be false, but psychological speech cannot be contrary to knowledge.
The response invokes inner experience (wijdan) to argue that we cannot judge contrary to what we know.
3
Consider the commentator's critique
The commentator notes that the response is debated because a person can verbally report what he does not know. However, the response may be specific to God.
The passage ends with 'so ponder' (tadabbar), indicating the commentator invites reflection.
4
Synthesize the lesson
Understand that the objection fails because the argument concerns psychological speech, not verbal speech. Psychological speech is internal and necessarily truthful.
This distinction is crucial for the overall argument about God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( واللازم باطل فإنا نعلم بالضرورة إلخ) فإن قلت هذا الدليل غير صحيح بجميع مقدماته إذ لو صح لدل على امتناع الصدق أيضا فإنه تعالى لو اتصف به لكان صدقه قديما فيلزم أن يمتنع عليه الكذب مع أنا نعلم أيضاً بالضرورة أن من علم شيئاً أمكن له أن يخبر عنه لا على عليه قلت : أجيب عنه بأن قوله : من علم شيئاً إلخ، ممنوع ودعوى الضرورة غير مسموعة، إذ ليس الكلام في الصدق والكذب اللفظيين حتى يمكنه ذلك بل في النفسانيين، ونحن نعلم بالوجدان أنا متى علمنا شيئاً فإنه يتعذر علينا أن نحكم بخلاف ما نعلمه، وهذا الجواب بعد ما قيل في بيان مغايرة مدلول الخبر للعلم من أن الرجل قد يخبر عما لا يعلمه بل يعلم خلافه محل بحث، ولو تم لدل من أول الأمر على امتناع قيام الكذب به ولا يحتاج إلى التشبث بلزوم امتناع الصدق بناء على أن ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه، اللهم إلا أن يحمل الجواب على المنع والسند ويكون المنع بالنظر إلى خصوص الباري سبحانه، فتأمل. قوله : ( إنما يدل على كون الكلام النفسي صدقاً دلالته على صدقه باعتبار تعلقاته النوعية القديمة ظاهرة، وأما باعتبار تعلقاته الشخصية الحادثة إن أثبتها الأشعري فمحل بحث . أن النفسي ( فلا) دلالة على صدقها لأنها حادثة فيجوز زوالها بحدوث الصدق الذي يقابلها، مع أن الأهم عندنا هو بيان صدقها
His statement: 'And the necessary consequence is false, for we know by necessity...' For if you say: this proof is not valid in all its premises; because if it were valid, it would also prove the impossibility of truth. For if He were described with truth, His truth would be eternal, and thus lying would be impossible for Him, while we also know by necessity that one who knows something can inform of it as it is. I say: it is answered that his statement 'one who knows something etc.' is denied, and the claim of necessity is not accepted, because the discussion is not about verbal truth and lying so that one could do that, but about psychological ones. And we know by inner experience that when we know something, it is impossible for us to judge contrary to our knowledge. This answer, after it was said in the explanation of the difference between the import of a report and knowledge (that a man may report what he does not know, indeed knows the opposite), is a matter of investigation. And if it were valid, it would from the very beginning prove the impossibility of lying in Him, and there would be no need to cling to the necessity of the impossibility of truth based on 'what is eternal cannot cease.' Unless the answer is taken as a denial of the premise, and the denial is with respect to the particularity of God the Exalted. So ponder. His statement: 'It only indicates that psychological speech is true; its indication of its truth with respect to its eternal generic relations is apparent. But with respect to its contingent particular relations, if al-Ash'ari affirms them, it is a matter of investigation.' As for the psychological speech, it has no indication of its truth because it is contingent, so its removal by the occurrence of truth opposite to it is permissible, although the most important for us is to show its truth.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage deals with an objection to the second argument. The objector says that the proof that lying is impossible because it would be eternal also proves that truth is impossible, for if truth were eternal, then lying would be impossible, but we know from necessity that one who knows something can inform of it as it is (implying truth is possible). The commentator responds by distinguishing verbal and psychological speech. The necessity mentioned applies to verbal speech, but the discussion here is about psychological speech (internal speech or belief). Regarding psychological speech, we know by inner experience that when we know something, we cannot judge its opposite. Thus, the objection is not valid. The commentator notes that this answer is debatable because sometimes a person reports what he does not know. However, if the answer were valid, it would directly prove the impossibility of lying in God without needing the argument about eternity. Then the commentator quotes the author's statement that the proof indicates the truth of psychological speech due to its eternal generic relations, but as for its contingent particular relations, it is debated. The passage concludes that psychological speech's truth is not indicated by the same proof because it is contingent, but we are more concerned with showing its truth.

Key Terms
الكلام النفسي
الكلام اللفظي
الصدق
الكذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument against lying also proves truth impossible, so the argument is self-defeating.
Correction: The argument only applies to psychological speech, which cannot be contrary to knowledge, so truth is not proven impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does the objection that the argument also proves truth impossible fail?
A) Because truth is not eternal
B) Because the argument only concerns psychological speech, not verbal speech
C) Because the necessity of knowledge does not apply to God
D) Because the objector misunderstands the premise

Key Takeaway
The objection that the argument would prove truth impossible fails because the argument concerns psychological speech, which cannot be contrary to knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you honestly say that your inner psychological state always matches your verbal statements? When is the last time you realized a contradiction between what you know and what you say, and what did you do about it?
?
If God's nature makes lying impossible for Him, then what does your own tendency to deceive—even in small ways—reveal about your distance from divine perfection?
?
The commentator casts doubt on the response, reminding us that even our understanding of divine truths is a matter of investigation. Are you willing to sit in that uncertainty, or do you demand easy answers that might be false?

Truth of Psychological Speech: Generic vs Particular Relations
Distinction between two types of relations (generic and particular) for psychological speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how psychological speech's truth is indicated through its generic relations, but not necessarily through its particular relations.

Core Idea
Psychological speech's truth is assured by its eternal generic relations, but contingent particular relations do not guarantee truth.

In the previous lesson, we saw that psychological speech is the focus. Now the author clarifies what gives psychological speech its truth. It distinguishes between two types of relations: generic (type) and particular (individual). The truth of psychological speech is clear with respect to its generic relations, but with particular relations, it is debatable. This lesson explores that nuance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two relations
Identify the two types of relations mentioned: generic (naw'iyya) and particular (shakhsiyya). Note that the author says the indication of truth is apparent for generic relations but debatable for particular relations.
Generic relations are eternal and unchanging, while particular relations are contingent and temporal.
2
Understand the significance of generic relations
Generic relations are eternal, so they guarantee the truth of psychological speech because they are linked to the type of knowledge.
The phrase 'eternal generic relations' refers to the universal aspect of God's knowledge.
3
Examine the exception for particular relations
Particular relations are contingent and subject to change, so the truth of psychological speech with respect to them is not assured. The author says it is a matter of investigation.
Al-Ash'ari's position on particular relations is mentioned as a point of debate.
4
Recognize the main goal
Despite the debate about particular relations, the most important point is to show the truth of psychological speech, which is achieved through generic relations.
The passage concludes that the primary concern is establishing the truth of psychological speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refuting Obligation to Know All Attributes
Refutes the obligation-to-know-all-attributes argument with two responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key responses to the argument that we must know all of Allah's attributes, showing that obligation is limited to what is necessary and does not require universal fulfillment.

Core Idea
Obligation is limited to our capacity, not to knowing every attribute.

This passage addresses a strong objection against the existence of additional attributes: if such attributes existed, we would be obligated to know them, but we don't. The commentator clarifies that 'addition' here means strength, not quantity. The response dismantles the objection by limiting the scope of obligation and acknowledging that even if full knowledge were obligatory, not everyone attains it. Let's examine the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objection: 'we are obligated to have complete knowledge of Him... so if He had an attribute other than them, we would know it.' Understand the logical chain.
2
Identify the first response
Look for the first answer: 'we deny the obligation of complete knowledge of Him, for it—i.e., obligation—is to the extent of our capacity.' Notice how it redefines the obligation.
The obligation is only for what is needed to affirm prophethood.
3
Identify the second response
Now find the second answer: 'we grant our obligation... but it does not follow from the obligation that it is attained by all those obliged.' This allows for partial fulfillment.
Prophets and saints may achieve complete knowledge, but not everyone.
4
Reflect on the overall reasoning
Consider how these two responses together refute the objection. The first limits the duty, the second allows for its non-fulfillment without contradiction.
The passage also clarifies that 'addition' means quality, not quantity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ومنهم من زاد على ذلك ليس المراد بالزيادة الزيادة بحسب الكمية بأن يقولوا بما قاله الأولون مع زيادة إذ لا معنى له قطعاً بل الزيادة بحسب الكيفية وهي القوة، وسيجيء نظيره في بحث أن الإيمان هل يزيد وينقص . ومنهم من زاد على ذلك فاستدل على نفيها بأن قال : نحن مكلفون بكمال المعرفة وإنما يحصل بمعرفة جميع صفاته فلو كان له صفة غيرها لعرفناها لكنا لا نعرفها بل لا طريق لنا إلى معرفة الصفات سوى الاستدلال بالأفعال والتنزيه عن النقائص، ولا يدل شيء منهما على صفة زائدة على ما ذكر والجواب منع التكليف بكمال معرفته إذ هو أي التكليف بقدر وسعنا فنحن مكلفون بأن نعرف من صفاته ما يتوقف تصديق النبي عليه السلام على العلم به لا بمعرفة صفات أخرى أو بأن نقول سلمنا تكليفنا بكمال معرفته لكن لا يلزم من التكليف به حصوله من جميع المكلفين بل ربما يعرفه معرفة كاملة بعض منهم كالأنبياء والكاملين من أتباعهم دون بعض وهو من عداهم، وهؤلاء وإن كانوا هم الأكثرين ولكن لا يمتنع كثرة الهالكين بسبب ترك ما كلفوا به من كمال معرفته .
His saying: 'And among them is one who added to that.' The intent is not addition in quantity, i.e., that they say what the first group said with an addition, for that is absolutely meaningless, but rather addition in quality, i.e., strength, and its parallel will come in the discussion of whether faith increases and decreases. And among them is one who added to that and used as evidence for its negation by saying: we are obligated to have complete knowledge of Him, and that is only attained by knowing all His attributes; so if He had an attribute other than them, we would know it, but we do not know it; rather, we have no path to knowledge of attributes except by inference from actions and transcendence above imperfections, and neither of these indicates an attribute additional to what was mentioned. And the answer: we deny the obligation of complete knowledge of Him, for it—i.e., obligation—is to the extent of our capacity. Thus we are obligated to know from His attributes what the affirmation of the Prophet, peace be upon him, depends on knowing, not to know other attributes. Or we say: we grant our obligation to have complete knowledge of Him, but it does not follow from the obligation that it is attained by all those obliged; rather, perhaps some of them—like the prophets and the perfect among their followers—know Him with complete knowledge, while others, i.e., those other than them, do not. And although these latter are the majority, there is no impossibility in the multitude of the perishing due to their abandoning what they were obligated to do regarding complete knowledge of Him.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents the second group's argument against additional attributes: they claim that since we are obligated to have complete knowledge of Allah, and complete knowledge requires knowing all His attributes, then if there were any attribute unknown to us, we would be in violation. But we do not know any attribute beyond the seven, and our only cognitive paths are inference from actions and transcendence—neither of which reveals extra attributes. The commentator first clarifies that 'addition' here means strength, not quantity. The refutation is twofold: (1) The obligation is within our capacity—we are only required to know what is necessary for affirming prophethood, not every attribute. (2) Even if complete knowledge were obligatory, its attainment is not necessary for all; some individuals (prophets and saints) may attain it, while the majority may fail, and it is possible that many perish due to neglect. This does not contradict divine wisdom.

Key Terms
كمال المعرفة
التكليف
وسع
تصديق النبي
هالكين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an obligation exists, everyone must fulfill it.
Correction: The passage shows that obligation does not entail universal compliance; some may fail and perish, which is not impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the first response to the argument that we must know all of Allah's attributes?
A) It is impossible to know any attribute.
B) The obligation is only to know what is necessary for affirming prophethood.
C) The prophets know all attributes, so the argument fails.
D) The attributes are unknowable in principle.

Key Takeaway
The objection that we must know all attributes fails because obligation is limited to our capacity and does not require universal fulfillment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your obligation is only to the extent of your capacity, what are you doing with the capacity you have? Are you using it to know what is necessary for affirming the Prophet, or are you neglecting it?
?
The passage says the majority may perish due to abandoning the obligation of complete knowledge. Are you content with being in the default of the many who do not strive for deeper knowledge, or will you pursue the path of the prophets and the perfect?
?
You claim to believe in Allah, but do you recognize that the minimal knowledge required is that which affirms the Prophet? Have you actually acquired that knowledge, or are you relying on blind acceptance?

The Effect of Power is Existence, Not Possibility
Definition and proof that qudra's effect is existence, not possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari argument denies that intrinsic possibility can be an effect of divine power, and why the effect of qudra is limited to existence (kawn).

Core Idea
Power creates existence; possibility is intrinsic and uncaused.

This lesson unpacks the initial argument against the need for an additional attribute of takwin. The author first distinguishes between soundness as intrinsic possibility and the actual coming-into-being. He argues that since intrinsic possibility cannot be caused, it cannot be the effect of power; rather, the effect of power is the existence of the possible. This eliminates the need to posit takwin as a separate attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice the claim and the answer.
Focus on the distinction between sihha and kawn.
2
Understand the two concepts
Identify sihha (soundness/possibility) and kawn (coming-into-being). Note that sihha is intrinsic to the possible thing.
3
Grasp why intrinsic possibility cannot be caused
The author states that intrinsic properties cannot be caused by another. This is key to the argument.
Relate to the principle 'ما بالذات لا يعلل بالغير'.
4
See the conclusion
The effect of power is kawn (existence), not possibility. Hence, no need for an extra attribute like takwin to produce existence.
This directly addresses the debate about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصحة لا تستلزم الكون، فلا يكون الكون أثراً لقدرة وأثر التكوين هو الكون. ( الجواب : إن الصحة الإمكان وأنه للممكن ذاتي فلا يصلح أثراً للقدرة) لأن ما بالذات لا يعلل بالغير بل به أي بإمكان الشيء في نفسه ) تعلل المقدورية فيقال : مقدور لأنه ممكن وذلك غير مقدور لأنه واجب أو ممتنع، فإذن أثر القدرة هو الكون ( أي كون المقدور وجوده لا صحته وإمكانه ) فاستغنى عن) إثبات (صفة) أخرى ( كذلك ) أي يكون أثرها الكون ( فإن قيل : المراد بالصحة) التي جعلناها أثراً للقدرة هو (صحة الفعل بمعنى التأثير والإيجاد من الفاعل ( لا صحة المفعول في نفسه)، وهذه الصحة إمكانه الذاتي الذي لا يمكن تعليله بغيره )، وأما الصحة الأولى فهي بالقياس إلى الفاعل ومعللة بالقدرة ) فإن القدرة هي الصفة التي باعتبارها يصح من الفاعل طرفا الفعل والترك ) على سواء من الشيء المقدور له، ( فلا يحصل بها ) منه ( أحدهما بعينه بل لا بد في حصوله من صفة أخرى متعلقة به أي بذلك الطرف وحده فتلك الصفة هي التكوين : قلنا : كل منهما ) أي من ذينك الطرفين ( يصلح أثراً لها أي للقدرة ( وإنما يحتاج صدور أحدهما) بعينه عنها (إلى مخصص ) بعينه ( وهو الإرادة ( المتعلقة بذلك الطرف، (و) حينئذ ( لا حاجة إلى مبدأ للكون غير القدرة ) المؤثرة فيه بواسطة الإرادة المتعلقة به
And soundness (al-sihha) does not entail coming-into-being (al-kawn), so coming-into-being would not be an effect of power (qudra) and the effect of bringing-into-being (al-takwin) is coming-into-being. The answer: Indeed soundness is possibility (al-imkan) and it is intrinsic to the possible, so it cannot be an effect of power. Because what is intrinsic cannot be caused by another, but rather by itself, i.e., by the thing's own possibility. The state of being possible (al-maqduriyya) is explained by it: one says 'it is possible because it is possible' and that is not possible because it is necessary or impossible. So the effect of power is coming-into-being, i.e., the existence of the possible, not its soundness/possibility. Thus there is no need to affirm another attribute likewise, whose effect is coming-into-being. If it is said: what is meant by soundness which we made an effect of power is the soundness of the act in the sense of causing an effect and bringing into existence on the part of the agent, not the soundness of the effect in itself, and that soundness is its intrinsic possibility which cannot be caused by another; as for the first soundness, it is relative to the agent and caused by power, for power is the attribute by which both sides of action and leaving are sound on the part of the agent, on equal footing regarding the thing intended to be within its power. So neither one of the two sides specifically occurs through it, rather there must be another attribute related to that side alone, and that attribute is bringing-into-being (al-takwin). We say: each of the two sides is fit to be an effect of it, i.e., of power, and what is needed for the occurrence of one specific side from it is a specific specifying factor, which is the will (al-irada) related to that side, and then there is no need for a principle of coming-into-being other than power, which effects it through the intermediary of will related to it.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents the core argument against the need for an additional attribute of takwin (bringing-into-being) alongside qudra (power). It is a definition and proof structured as an objection-and-answer. The author begins by stating that soundness (sihha) of possibility does not necessitate the actual coming-into-being (kawn) of a thing. Therefore, if one claims that the effect of power is the coming-into-being, and that this requires an additional attribute (takwin), the author responds by distinguishing between intrinsic possibility (which belongs to the possible thing itself) and the act of bringing into existence. The key point: intrinsic possibility cannot be caused by another; it is a property of the thing's essence. So the effect of power is not possibility but the actual existence (kawn) of the possible. Then an objection is raised: perhaps 'soundness' refers to the agent's capacity to act, not the thing's possibility. The author replies that even so, power alone only gives the ability to do or not do, but to bring about a specific act requires a specifying factor (mukhassis) which is the will (irada), not a separate attribute of takwin. Thus, the need for takwin is eliminated; qudra, with the mediation of irada, is sufficient to produce the existence of the thing. This argument defends the Ash'ari position that takwin is not a distinct attribute.

Key Terms
صحة (sihha: soundness/possibility)
كون (kawn: coming-into-being/existence)
قدرة (qudra: power)
تكوين (takwin: bringing-into-being)
إمكان (imkan: possibility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'soundness' (sihha) refers to the possibility of a thing being created, and that this possibility is an effect of divine power.
Correction: The author clarifies that sihha here means intrinsic possibility (imkan) of the possible thing, which is not caused by another; it is a self-evident property.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that intrinsic possibility (imkan) cannot be an effect of power?
A) Because possibility is a created accident.
B) Because intrinsic properties cannot be caused by another.
C) Because power can only produce existence.
D) Because possibility is the same as existence.

Key Takeaway
The effect of divine power is the existence of the possible, not its intrinsic possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is directly caused by God's power and not just a possibility, how should that shape your gratitude and dependence on Him today?
?
When you consider that your own potential and abilities are merely intrinsic possibilities, not caused by you, does that humble your pride? What specific action can you take to acknowledge that only God's power actualizes your actions?
?
Do you ever act as if your own power creates possibilities? How can you realign your mindset to see that you only exist and act because God's power brings your existence moment by moment?
