Power Plus Will Sufice, No Need for Takwin
Objection and rebuttal regarding the need for takwin based on agent's capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that the agent's capacity requires a separate attribute of takwin is rebutted by the role of will as the specifying factor.

Core Idea
Will, not a separate takwin, specifies which act occurs.

In this lesson, we examine an objection: perhaps 'soundness' refers to the agent's capacity to act, which is caused by power, but to bring about a specific act requires an additional attribute called takwin. The author replies that power can produce either action or inaction; the specific one is determined by the divine will (irada). Thus, takwin is unnecessary; qudra with irada suffices.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the objection (the claim that soundness means agent's capacity) and the answer (will as specifying factor).
Notice the phrase 'فإن قيل' (if it is said) marking the objection.
2
Understand the two meanings of soundness
The objection distinguishes between soundness of the act (agent's capacity) and soundness of the effect (intrinsic possibility). The first is caused by power.
3
See the role of will
The author argues that power alone gives capacity for either action or inaction; the specific one requires a specifying factor (mukhassis), which is the will (irada).
The will is related to that specific side.
4
Conclude: no need for takwin
Since will suffices to specify, there is no need for an additional attribute (takwin) to produce the act. Qudra and irada together are sufficient.

Philosophers' Arguments and Sunni Refutation on Vision
Philosophers argue vision is eye-impression; Sunni refutes with three examples and concludes contrary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Sunni tradition refutes the philosophers' claim that vision is merely a physical impression on the eye, and why this matters for the possibility of seeing Allah.

Core Idea
Vision is not just an eye-impression; it is a direct creation of Allah.

The philosophers present three empirical examples to argue that vision is reducible to a physical impression on the pupil. The Sunni response accepts that the eye is affected during sight, but denies that this impression constitutes the act of seeing itself. This distinction preserves the possibility of the Beatific Vision of Allah, which would not depend on physical conditions like light or facing.

Guided Path
1
Read the full Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the structure: initial distinction, philosophers' claim, three examples, then the refutation.
2
Identify the three examples
Find the three examples: sun afterimage, green meadow afterimage, and overpowering light/whiteness. Understand what each is meant to prove.
Each example is supposed to show that an impression persists in the eye after the object is gone.
3
Understand the Sunni refutation
The Sunni acknowledges impression but denies it is the same as vision. Vision is an additional matter created by Allah, not bound by physical conditions.
The refutation does not deny the empirical data, but reinterprets it.
4
Connect to the broader doctrine
Reflect on why this matters: if vision were just physical impression, seeing Allah would be impossible since He is not material. The refutation keeps the door open for the Beatific Vision in the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للحالة الأولى التي هي الرؤية بالضرورة، فإن الحالتين وإن اشتركتا في حصول العلم فيهما إلا أن الحالة الأولى فيها أمر زائد هو الرؤية، وكذا إذا علمنا شيئاً علماً تاماً جلياً ثم رأيناه فإنا نعلم بالبديهة تفرقة بين الحالتين، وأن في الثانية زيادة ليست في الأولى ( قالت الفلاسفة هي ( أي تلك المغايرة والزيادة عائدة إلى تأثر الحدقة لا إلى زيادة الانكشاف هي الرؤية والإبصار ( الوجوه : الأول : إن من نظر إلى الشمس بالاستقصاء ثم غمض فإنه يتخيل أن الشمس حاضرة عنده لا يتأتى له أن يدفعه)، أي هذا التخيل ) عن نفسه أصلا وما ذلك إلا لأن الحدقة تأثرت عن صورة الشمس وبقيت صورتها في الحدقة بعد أن زالت الرؤية . ( الثاني : إن من (نظر) بالاستقصاء (إلى روضة خضراء زماناً طويلاً ( ثم حول عينيه إلى شيء أبيض ) فإنه ( يرى لونه ممتزجاً من البياض والخضرة فقد تحقق أن حدقته تأثرت عن الخضرة وبقى صورتها فيها بعد التحول الثالث أن الضوء القوي يقهر الباصرة ) وكذلك البياض الشديد يقهرها بحيث لو نظر الرائي بعد رؤيتهما إلى ضوء ضعيف أو بياض ضعيف لم يرهما ( فلولا تأثرها ) أي تأثر الحاسة ( منه ) بل منهما ( لما كان ( الأمر ( كذلك قلنا : كل ذلك ) الذي ذكرتموه ( يدل على تأثر (الحدقة عند الإبصار ( وأما عود) تلك الزيادة التي هي ) الإبصار إليه ) أي إلى التأثر ( فلا ) دلالة عليه فلا هي ( أي فلا الإبصار بتأويل الرؤية ( هو ) أي تأثر الحاسة (ولا) هي ( مشروطة به عندنا فجاز أن يرى الله سبحانه وتعالى من غير أن تتأثر عنه الحاسة ) وقد سبق ما فيه كفاية وهو أن الرؤية أمر يخلقه الله في الحي ولا يشترط بضوء ولا مقابلة ولا غيرهما من الشرائط التي اعتبرها الحكماء
As for the first state, which is vision necessarily; for the two states, even though they share in obtaining knowledge in them, yet the first state has an extra matter, which is vision. Similarly, when we know something with complete clear knowledge and then see it, we know by intuition a distinction between the two states, and that in the second there is an addition not in the first. (The philosophers said: That difference and addition is due to the impression on the pupil, not to an increase of disclosure; it is vision and sight. The aspects: First: One who gazes at the sun exhaustively then closes his eyes imagines that the sun is present before him, unable to repel it from himself at all, and that is only because the pupil was impressed by the image of the sun and its image remained in the pupil after vision ceased. Second: One who looks exhaustively at a green meadow for a long time then turns his eyes to a white thing sees its color mixed with whiteness and greenness; so it is confirmed that his pupil was impressed by the greenness and its image remained in it after the turn. Third: Strong light overpowers the sight, and likewise intense whiteness overpowers it, such that if the viewer after seeing them looks at weak light or weak whiteness he does not see them. For if not for its impression (i.e., the sense's impression) from it, rather from both, the matter would not be so. We say: All that which you mentioned indicates the impression of the pupil at the time of sight. As for the return of that addition, which is sight, to it (i.e., to the impression), there is no indication of it. So sight (i.e., vision) is not the impression of the sense, nor is it conditional upon it with us. Thus it is permissible that Allah, exalted, be seen without the sense being impressed by Him. And there has already preceded what suffices: that vision is a matter that Allah creates in the living being, and it is not conditional upon light, nor facing, nor any other of the conditions considered by the philosophers.)
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument that vision is reducible to a physical impression on the eye, and then refutes it from a Sunni perspective. It begins by describing the intuitive distinction between mere knowledge and vision: knowledge is one thing, vision adds something extra. The philosophers claim that this extra is just the impression of the object on the pupil. They give three examples: (1) after staring at the sun and closing the eyes, one still sees an afterimage, which they say is due to the pupil retaining the impression; (2) after staring at green and then looking at white, the white appears mixed with green, again showing retention; (3) strong light or whiteness overwhelms the sight, preventing weaker stimuli from being seen. These are all meant to prove that vision involves a physical impression persisting in the eye. The Sunni response acknowledges that these examples show the pupil is affected during sight, but denies that this impression is what vision is. The addition of sight (the act of seeing) is not proven to be the same as that impression. Therefore, vision can be something else—something directly created by Allah—without requiring the conditions of light, facing, or optical anatomy. This prepares for the Sunni doctrine on the Beatific Vision.

Key Terms
الرؤية
الإبصار
تأثر الحدقة
الانكشاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the philosophers' three examples prove that vision is exactly a physical impression on the eye, leaving no room for a non-physical act of seeing.
Correction: The examples only prove that the eye is affected during vision, but they do not prove that vision itself is that impression. Vision is an additional act created by Allah, not reducible to physics.

Checkpoint
According to the Sunni refutation, what do the three examples (sun afterimage, green mixing, light overpowering) actually prove?
A) That vision is identical to the physical impression on the pupil
B) That the pupil is affected during sight, but vision is something else created by Allah
C) That vision requires the presence of light and facing
D) That the philosophers are correct in all their claims

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' arguments prove the eye is affected during vision, but they do not prove that vision itself is that physical impression, allowing for a divinely created act of sight unbound by physical conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that Allah sees you without being physically impressed or conditioned like your own sight, does that awareness change how you feel about being watched? What would you do differently today if you truly lived as if His vision of you is not a passive observation but an active, creative act that sees you right now?
?
If vision is not reducible to physical processes, then your own seeing each moment is a direct gift from Allah, recreated every instant. Have you ever thanked Him simply for the ability to see? What would it mean for you to stop taking your sight for granted and to acknowledge it as a continuous act of divine creation?
?
The philosophers demanded physical conditions for seeing Allah, but the Sunni position frees you from limiting Allah by your own physicality. Is there a part of your life where you have unconsciously conditioned Allah's involvement based on your physical circumstances or senses? What would it look like to trust that He can act without any of the conditions you assume are necessary?

The Objection: Did Musa Ask for Sight or Knowledge?
Presents objection and linguistic refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the kalam argument and why the linguistic analysis of the preposition 'ila' refutes the figurative interpretation of Musa's request.

Core Idea
The word 'ila' in 'arini' forces a visual meaning, not a metaphorical one.

The Mu'tazilite theologian Abu Hudhayl al-'Allaf argued that when Musa said 'arini' (show me), he actually meant 'make me know You with necessary knowledge,' not literal sight. This lesson examines that objection and the first counter-argument: that the Arabic preposition 'ila' consistently points to physical sight in such constructions, making the figurative reading untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and figurative interpretation
Read the passage where the objection is stated: Musa's request is interpreted as seeking necessary knowledge, a common Mu'tazilite ta'wil.
Note the names: Abu Hudhayl al-'Allaf, al-Jubba'i, and the Basrans.
2
Examine the first refutation
Focus on the argument that 'ra'a' with 'ila' is very far from meaning knowledge. The preposition is key.
Also consider 'nazar' with 'ila'.
3
Understand the principle of apparent meaning
Contravening the apparent (al-zahir) requires evidence. Since there is none, the literal interpretation of sight must be upheld.
This is a core principle in both kalam and usul al-fiqh.

الاعتراض أما على الأول فمن وجوه الأول : أن موسى عليه السلام لم يسأل الرؤية بل تجوز بها عن العلم ) : الضروري لأنه لازمها وإطلاق اسم الملزوم وعلى اللازم شائع )، سيما استعمال رأى بمعنى علم وأرى بمعنى أعلم فكأنه قال : اجعلني عالماً بك علماً ضرورياً، وهذا تأويل أبي الهذيل العلاف وتبعه) فيه ( الجبائي وأكثر البصريين والجواب أن الرؤية وإن استعملت للعلم لكنها إذا وصلت بإلى فبعيد جدا، والصواب أن يقال : لو كانت الرؤية المطلوبة في أرني بمعنى العلم لكان النظر المترتب عليه بمعناه أيضاً، والنظر وإن استعمل بمعنى العلم إلا أن استعماله فيه موصولاً بإلى مستبعد مخالف للظاهر قطعاً ) ومخالفة الظاهر لا تجوز إلا (لدليل ولا دليل هاهنا فوجب حمله على الرؤية حمله على الرؤية بل على تقليب الحدقة نحو المرئي المؤدي إلى رؤيته فيكون الطلب للرؤية أيضاً، ( ثم) نقول : ( يمتنع (حملها أي حمل الرؤية المطلوبة ( عليه ) أي على العلم الضروري ( هاهنا ، أما أولا فلأنه يلزم أن لا يكون موسى عالماً بربه ضرورة مع أنه يخاطبه وذلك لا يعقل لأن المخاطب في حكم الحاضر المشاهد وما هو معلوم بالنظر ليس كذلك ، ( وأما ثانياً فلأن الجواب ينبغي أن يطابق السؤال، وقوله : لن تراني نفي للرؤية ) لا للعلم الضروري ( بإجماع المعتزلة فلو حمل السؤال على طلب العلم لم يطابقا أصلاً . قوله : (بل على تقليب (الحدقة فإن قلت : هذا ليس كما لأنه ينبغي بالنسبة إليه تعالى قلت : مراده أن مقتضى اللفظ والتعدية بإلى الحمل على التقليب لكون النظر الموصول بإلى حقيقة فيه وأما امتناعه بالنسبة إليه تعالى فيندفع بجعل اللفظ كناية عن لازمه ومؤداه، أعني الرؤية هذا غاية ما يقال وفيه نظر لأن اللفظ في الكناية ليس بمستعمل في الموضوع له عند الشارح كما حققه في شرح المفتاح. قوله : ( ضرورة مع أنه يخاطبه أورد عليه أن المراد هو العلم بهويته الخاصة والخطاب لا يقتضي إلا العلم بوجه كمن يخاطبنا من وراء الجدار وأجيب بأن العلم بالهوية الخاصة بمعنى الانكشاف التام لا تكون إلا بالمشاهدة والعيان كما هو شأن جميع الجزئيات الحقيقية فيؤول إلى الرؤية وبمعنى العلم بكنه حقيقته الجزئيات ليس بلازم للرؤية فلا يصح قولهم : بل تجوز بها عن العلم الضروري، لأنه لازمها نعم يراد أن يقال خطاب موسى عليه السلام إياه تعالى لا يفيد العلم به ضرورة، ألا يرى أن تخاطبه تعالى مع أن وجوده تعالي ثابت عندنا بالبرهان لا بالضرورة، وأما خطابه تعالى إياه فهو بمعنى خلق لفظ غیر قائم به ودلالة المخلوق على الخالق نظري اللهم إلا أن يقال : سرعة الانتقال من الأثر إلى المؤثر يلحقه بالضروري فليتأمل. قوله : ( بإجماع المعتزلة) لعله يريد إجماعهم قبل ظهور الزمخشري وإلا فقد قال هو في کشافه : لن تراني أي لن تطيق معرفتي بهذه الطريقة .
The objection as for the first [proof] is from aspects, the first: that Musa, peace be upon him, did not ask for vision (al-ru'ya) but used it figuratively for knowledge: necessary knowledge because it is its concomitant, and applying the name of the concomitant to the thing is common, especially the usage of 'ra'a' meaning 'knowledge' and 'ara' meaning 'I make known', so as if he said: make me know You with necessary knowledge. This is the interpretation (ta'wil) of Abu Hudhayl al-'Allaf, and al-Jubba'i and most of the Basrans followed him in it. The answer: that vision (al-ru'ya), even if it is used for knowledge, yet when it is connected with 'ila', it is very far [from that meaning]. The correct is that if the requested vision in 'arini' meant knowledge, then the looking (nazar) entailed by it would also be in that meaning, and looking, even if used for knowledge, yet its usage in it connected with 'ila' is far-fetched, undoubtedly contrary to the apparent. And contravening the apparent is not permissible except with evidence, and there is no evidence here, so it must be carried upon vision. Carried upon vision, but rather upon the turning of the pupil toward the visible that leads to seeing it, so the request is also for vision. Then we say: carrying it—i.e., carrying the requested vision—upon it—i.e., upon necessary knowledge—is impossible here. First, because it would necessitate that Musa is not knower of his Lord necessarily while he is addressing Him, and that is unreasonable, because the addressed is in the ruling of the present witness, and what is known through reflection is not like that. Second, because the answer must match the question. And His saying 'lan tarani' is a negation of vision, not of necessary knowledge, by consensus of the Mu'tazila; if the question were interpreted as seeking knowledge, they would not match at all. His saying: 'but upon the turning of the pupil' — if you say: this is not correct because it is not permissible with respect to Him, Exalted — I say: his intent is that the requirement of the wording and the transitivity with 'ila' is to carry it upon turning, because looking connected with 'ila' is real in it. As for its impossibility with respect to Him, Exalted, it is repelled by making the wording a metonymy (kinaya) for its necessary consequence and import, namely vision. This is the utmost that can be said, and there is consideration in it because the wording in metonymy is not used in its assigned meaning according to the commentator as he verified in Sharh al-Miftah. His saying: 'necessarily while he is addressing Him' — it was objected to him that what is meant is knowledge of His specific identity (huwiyya), and address does not necessitate except knowledge of a face, like one who addresses us from behind a wall. And it was answered that knowledge of the specific identity in the sense of complete disclosure is only through direct sight and vision, as is the case with all real particulars, so it leads to vision; and in the sense of knowledge of the essence of His true nature, it is not a necessary concomitant of vision, so their saying 'but he used it figuratively for necessary knowledge because it is its concomitant' is not correct. Rather, it is intended to be said that the address of Musa, peace be upon him, to Him does not convey knowledge of Him necessarily. Do you not see that we address Him while His existence is established to us by proof, not necessarily? As for His address to him, it is in the sense of creating a word not subsisting in Him, and the indication of the created to the Creator is theoretical; unless it is said: the speed of transition from the effect to the effective cause makes it attach to the necessary — so reflect. His saying: 'by consensus of the Mu'tazila' — perhaps he intends their consensus before the emergence of al-Zamakhshari, otherwise he said in his Kashshaf: 'lan tarani' means you will not be able to know Me through this method.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the first objection against the first proof. The objection, attributed to Abu Hudhayl al-'Allaf and followed by al-Jubba'i and the Basrans, claims that Musa's request 'arini' is figurative for seeking necessary knowledge, not literal sight. The author gives two responses. First, the word 'ra'a' when used transitively with 'ila' (as in 'unzur ilayya' and 'arini') strongly implies visual sight, not knowledge; using it for knowledge would be a departure from the apparent meaning, which requires evidence. Second, the response 'lan tarani' negates vision, not knowledge, by consensus of the Mu'tazila themselves; if the question were about knowledge, the answer would not match. The passage then includes extensive glosses. One gloss clarifies that 'nazar' with 'ila' is literally turning the pupil, but since that is impossible for God, it is a metonymy for vision. Another gloss argues that if Musa had necessary knowledge, he would not need to ask; the fact that he asked shows he did not have it. A final gloss notes a variant in al-Zamakhshari's interpretation of 'lan tarani' as inability to know, but this is a later opinion.

Key Terms

العلم الضروري
التجوز
الظاهر
إلى
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that Musa's request 'arini' was a metaphor for seeking necessary knowledge of God.
Correction: The linguistic analysis of the preposition 'ila' shows that the phrase literally implies visual sight, and departing from the apparent meaning requires evidence, which is absent.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the interpretation that 'arini' means 'make me know' in this passage?
A) Because 'ra'a' never means knowledge in Arabic.
B) Because the preposition 'ila' makes the literal sense of vision dominant, and there is no evidence to support the figurative reading.
C) Because the Mu'tazila unanimously rejected that interpretation.
D) Because Musa already had necessary knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The figurative interpretation of Musa's request fails because the preposition 'ila' anchors the meaning to literal vision, and no evidence supports the departure from the apparent meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'arini' in Surah al-A'raf, do you instinctively want to soften its demand? What does this passage’s insistence on literal sight reveal about your own comfort with God’s transcendence? Will you accept the apparent meaning, or will you rationalize it away to avoid discomfort?
?
Musa, the prophet to whom God spoke directly, still asked to see Him. Do you truly yearn for the vision of God as Musa did, or has your spiritual desire become diluted with worldly pursuits? How will you rekindle that longing today?
?
This lesson argues that the language of the Quran is precise and must be taken literally unless proven otherwise. Are you willing to submit to the zahir of divine speech even when it challenges your intellect or preconceived notions? What specific verse or teaching will you commit to accepting on its face, starting now?

The Second Refutation: Matching Question and Answer
Second refutation and additional glosses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second refutation of the figurative interpretation, showing that the divine response 'lan tarani' negates vision and that Musa could not have been asking for necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
The answer must match the question: 'lan tarani' negates sight, not knowledge.

The Mu'tazilite claim that Musa asked for necessary knowledge fails on multiple fronts. In this lesson, we examine the second refutation: that the Quranic response 'lan tarani' (you will not see Me) explicitly negates vision, and the Mu'tazila themselves confirm this. Additionally, the argument that asking for necessary knowledge would imply Musa lacked such knowledge at the moment of addressing God is shown to be problematic.

Guided Path
1
Read the second refutation's two points
First, if Musa had necessary knowledge, he wouldn't need to ask. Second, God's answer 'lan tarani' negates vision, not knowledge.
The Mu'tazila themselves agree that 'lan tarani' negates vision.
2
Examine the gloss on metonymy
The turning of the pupil is impossible for God, so it is treated as a metonymy (kinaya) for the resulting vision.
This resolves the apparent impossibility.
3
Analyze the discussion on necessary knowledge
The gloss debates whether addressing God gives necessary knowledge. It argues it does not, as we know God by proof, not necessity.
Speed of transition might make it seem necessary.
4
Note al-Zamakhshari's variant
Al-Zamakhshari interpreted 'lan tarani' as inability to know, but this is a later opinion.
The consensus before him was clear.

?

?

Length and Breadth as Non-Accidents in Atomism
Al-Jurjani argues that length and breadth are not accidents due to atomic composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Jurjani's argument that length and breadth are not accidents due to the atomistic composition of bodies, and how he defends this against objections.

Core Idea
Length and breadth cannot be accidents because atoms cannot host them without contradiction.

This lesson delves into a subtle argument from kalam atomism. Al-Jurjani presents a claim that length and breadth are not accidents inhering in the body, based on the body's composition of indivisible atoms. The reasoning is tight: if length were in one atom, that atom would be larger than another, contradicting indivisibility; if in multiple atoms, the same accident would exist in two loci, which is impossible. We'll walk through the argument and its responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes a main argument, a response, and a marginal note—all connected.
2
Identify the two cases in the argument
The author presents two possibilities for length subsisting in atoms: in one atom or in multiple atoms. Find them and see how each leads to a contradiction.
The first case leads to divisibility, the second to impossibility of one accident in two loci.
3
Examine the response (يجاب)
The response clarifies what 'distinction' means in vision—it is by mere sight without external factors. The example of the blind and the amputee shows the need for reason.
This response is not directly about the main argument but about how we perceive length and breadth.
4
Analyze the marginal note (قوله)
This section refines the objection about one accident in two loci. It considers the possibility that the locus is the whole composite, not each part separately.
The author replies that the impossibility is clear from the first case, and the hesitation is not exhaustive.
5
Synthesize the overall argument
Combine the parts: the main argument contradicts the idea of length/breadth as accidents, while the responses and refinements safeguard the position.
Reflect on how atomism shapes the entire discussion.

المرصد الخامس - المقصد الأول : في الرؤية والكلام
العرض ونميز الطويل من الأطول وليس الطول والعرض عرضين قائمين بالجسم لما تقرر من أنه مركب من الجواهر الفردة فالطول مثلا إن قام بجزء واحد منها فذلك الجزء يكون أكثر حجما من جزء آخر فيقبل القسمة هذا خلف وإن قام بأكثر من جزء واحد لزم قيام العرض الواحد بمحلين وهو محال
يجاب : بأن المراد هو التميز بمجرد استعمال البصر من غير أن يكون لأمر آخر مدخل فيه وتمييز الأعمى من الأقطع حيث هو ذلك يحتاج إلى معاونة العقل وراء المعاونة الأساسية، وأنت خبير بأن الكلام في ثبوت الفرق فتدبر.
قوله : ( لزم قيام العرض الواحد بمحلين وهو محال فإن قلت : لعل للطول جزءاً خارجاً قائماً بجزء المحل . قلت : جزء الطول طول على ما اعترف به المثبتون لاقتضاء أصلهم فيعود المحذور ، نعم يرد عليه أن المحال قيام العرض الواحد بمحلين على أن يكون كل منهما محلاً على حدة، وأما إذا كان المحل هو المجموع كما هو المفروض هنا فلا فإن قلت : المفروض قيامه بكل واحد كما في الافتراق والاجتماع قلت : فالتردد غير حاصر على أن بطلان هذا علم من الشق الأول .
The Fifth Observatory - First Objective: On Vision and Speech. (We mean) breadth and we distinguish the long from the longer. And length and breadth are not two accidents subsisting in the body, because it has been established that it (the body) is composed of individual atoms. For example, if length subsists in one part of them, then that part would be larger in volume than another part, and thus it would be divisible—this is a contradiction. And if it subsists in more than one part, then the subsistence of one accident in two loci would be necessary, which is impossible. Response: What is meant is distinction by mere use of sight without any other matter having an input in it. And the distinction of the blind from the amputee, as it is, requires the aid of reason beyond the basic aid. And you are aware that the discussion is about the establishment of the difference, so reflect. His saying: (The subsistence of one accident in two loci would be necessary, which is impossible.) For if you say: Perhaps length has an external part subsisting in a part of the locus. I say: A part of length is length, according to what the affirmers have conceded because their principle requires it, so the difficulty returns. Yes, it may be objected that what is impossible is the subsistence of one accident in two loci in such a way that each is a locus separately. But as for when the locus is the whole, as is assumed here, then not. For if you say: The assumption is its subsistence in each one, as in separation and conjunction. I say: The hesitation is not exhaustive, and that the invalidity of this is known from the first side.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage contains a concentrated argument and a gloss. The author begins by clarifying the topic: the Fifth Observatory, First Objective concerns vision and speech. He then presents an argument that length and breadth are not accidents inhering in the body. The reasoning is based on atomism: the body is composed of indivisible atoms (jawahir fardah). If length were an accident subsisting in a single atom, that atom would be larger in volume than another atom, making it divisible—a contradiction. If length subsisted in more than one atom, then the same accident would subsist in two distinct loci, which is impossible because an accident cannot inhere in multiple substrates. The next segment, a gloss introduced by "يجاب" (It is responded), clarifies the meaning of 'distinction' (tamyiz) in vision: it is a distinction achieved solely by the use of sight without other factors. The example of distinguishing a blind person from an amputee shows that even such basic distinctions require the aid of reason beyond mere sensation. The gloss concludes that the discussion is about establishing the difference, not about the definition of vision per se. The following marginal note (قوله) further refines the argument against the possibility of an accident subsisting in two loci. If one suggests that length has a part standing in a part of the locus, the reply is that a part of length is itself length, so the same problem recurs. An objector might say that impossibility applies only when each is an independent locus, not when the locus is the whole composite. But the author responds that this hesitation does not exhaust the possibilities, and the invalidity is already clear from the first line of reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Length and breadth are obviously accidents that inhere in bodies.
Correction: In atomist kalam, they cannot be accidents because that leads to contradictions; they are relational notions.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that length cannot be an accident subsisting in a single atom?
A) Because atoms are indivisible and cannot have accidents.
B) Because that would make the atom larger than another, implying divisibility.
C) Because length is a substance, not an accident.
D) Because accidents can only subsist in composites.

Key Takeaway
In atomist kalam, attributing length or breadth to a body as accidents leads to contradictions, showing they must be understood differently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that even something as seemingly simple as 'length' can be philosophically problematic. What is one assumption about the physical world that you have never questioned? Are you willing to examine it with the same rigor Al-Jurjani applied here?
?
The author uses precise reasoning to reject a common-sense view. Is there a belief about Allah's attributes that you hold without having thought through its logical implications? What would it cost you to investigate that belief more deeply?
?
The gloss mentions that distinction requires the aid of reason beyond sight. In your own life, when have you relied on reason to correct what your senses or intuition told you? What does that say about the importance of systematic theology ('ilm al-kalām)?

Existence as the Common Cause of Visibility
Al-Jurjani proves that existence is the common cause for visibility of substances, accidents, and God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jurjani argues that existence, not contingency, is the common cause that makes both substances and accidents (and ultimately God) visible.

Core Idea
Only existence can be the cause of visibility because contingency includes non-existence, which cannot cause.

In this passage, al-Jurjani builds on the earlier proof that visibility is possible for both substances and accidents. He now investigates why that possibility is actualized when things exist and not when they don't. The argument leads to a crucial conclusion: the common cause must be existence itself, which applies even to God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
The possibility of vision is shared by substances and accidents, but it is only actual when they exist. Why? There must be a cause that explains this specification.
Without a cause, the specification would be an arbitrary preference.
2
Identify the two candidates
The cause must be common to both substances and accidents. Al-Jurjani identifies two possible common attributes: existence (wujud) and contingency (huduth).
These are the only general attributes shared by all created things.
3
See why contingency fails
Contingency is defined as existence with prior non-existence. Non-existence cannot be part of a cause because causation is a positive attribute. Thus contingency is invalid.
The author also notes that non-existence can be a condition but not a part of the efficient cause.
4
Conclude: existence is the cause
Once contingency is eliminated, only existence remains as the common cause. Since existence is shared by all existents, including God, the possibility of seeing God is established.
This completes the proof that God can be seen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد ثبت أن صحة الرؤية مشتركة بين الجوهر والعرض وهذه الصحة لها علة مختصة بحال وجودهما، وذلك لتحققها عند الوجود كما عرفت وانتفائها عند العدم فإن الأجسام والأعراض لو كانت معدومة لاستحال كونها مرئية بالضرورة والاتفاق ولولا تحقق أمر مصحح حال الوجود غير متحقق حال العدم لكان ذلك أي اختصاص الصحة بحال الوجود ترجيحاً بلا مرجح لأن نسبة الصحة على تقدير استغنائها عن العلة إلى طرفي الوجود والعدم على سواء وهذه العلة المصححة للرؤية لا بد من أن تكون مشتركة بين الجوهر والعرض وإلا لزم تعليل الأمر الواحد وهو صحة كون الشيء مرئياً بالعلل المختلفة وهي الأمور المختصة إما بالجواهر وإما بالأعراض وهو غير جائز لما مر في مباحث العلل ثم نقول : هذه العلة المشتركة إما الوجود أو الحدث إذ لا مشترك بين الجوهر والعرض سواهما فإن الأجسام لا توافق الألوان في صفة عامة بتوهم كونها مصححة سوى هذين لكن الحدوث لا يصلح أن يكون علة للصحة لأنه عبارة عن الوجود مع اعتبار عدم سابق والعدم لا يصلح أن يكون جزءاً للعلة لأن التأثير صفة إثبات فلا يتصف به العدم ولا ما هو مركب منه وإذا أسقط العدم عن درجة الاعتبار لم يبق إلا الوجود فإذن هي أي العلة المشتركة الوجود وإنه مشترك بينهما وبين الواجب لما تقدم من اشتراك الوجود بين الموجودات كلها فعلة صحة الرؤية متحققة لله تعالى فيتحقق صحة الرؤية وهو المطلوب.
قوله : ( لما مر في مباحث العلل فيه أن المراد بالعلة هاهنا هو المؤثر وهاهنا متعلق الرؤية كما سيصرح به وبطلان تعليل أمر واحد يعلل بذلك المعنى لا يفيد بطلان تعليله بعلل بهذا المعنى . قوله : ( والعدم لا يصلح أن يكون جزءاً من العلة) فيه أنه لا يمتنع الشرطية مع أنهم قائلون بأن العدم يكون جزءاً من العلة التامة وإن لم يكن جزءاً من المؤثر في الوجود.
It has been established that the possibility of vision is shared between substance and accident, and this possibility has a cause specific to the state of their existence. That is because it is actualized at existence, as you have learned, and absent at non-existence. For if bodies and accidents were non-existent, it would be impossible for them to be visible by necessity and agreement. And if there were no actualizing factor present at existence and not present at non-existence, then that—i.e., the specification of possibility to the state of existence—would be a preference without a preferrer, because the relation of possibility to the two sides of existence and non-existence is equal, on the assumption that it is independent of a cause. And this cause that makes vision possible must be common between substance and accident; otherwise, it would necessitate explaining one thing—i.e., the possibility of a thing being visible—by different causes, which are matters specific either to substances or to accidents, and that is not permissible, as has passed in the discussions of causes. Then we say: This common cause is either existence or contingency, since there is no common factor between substance and accident other than these two. For bodies do not agree with colors in any general attribute supposed to be a cause except these two. But contingency is not fit to be a cause for possibility, because it is existence with the consideration of prior non-existence, and non-existence is not fit to be part of the cause, because effect [literally: influence] is a positive attribute, so non-existence is not qualified by it, nor is what is composed of it. And when non-existence is removed from consideration, nothing remains except existence. So it—i.e., the common cause—is existence, and it is shared between them (substance and accident) and the Necessary (God), because of what has preceded about existence being shared among all existents. Thus the cause of the possibility of vision is realized for God Almighty, so the possibility of vision is realized, and that is what is sought. His saying: (As has passed in the discussions of causes) in it is that what is meant by cause here is the efficient cause, and here it is the object of vision as he will clarify, and the invalidity of explaining one thing by causes in that sense does not entail the invalidity of explaining it by causes in this sense. His saying: (And non-existence is not fit to be part of the cause) in it is that conditionality is not impossible, even though they say that non-existence is part of the complete cause, even if it is not part of the efficient cause in existence.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a proof establishing that existence (al-wujud) is the common cause (al-illa al-mushtaraka) for the visibility of both substances and accidents, and by extension for God. The argument begins by noting that the possibility (sihha) of vision is common to substances and accidents. This possibility is actualized when they exist and absent when they do not. If no cause were needed, then the specification of possibility to existence rather than non-existence would be an arbitrary preference without a preferrer, which is impossible. Therefore, there must be a cause that makes visibility possible, and this cause must be common to both substances and accidents; otherwise, the same effect (visibility) would have different causes, which is not permissible (as established in discussions on causality). The common cause can only be existence or contingency (huduth), because these are the only shared attributes between substance and accident. Contingency is dismissed because it includes non-existence (prior non-existence), and non-existence cannot be part of a cause since causation is a positive attribute. Thus, only existence remains as the common cause. Existence is shared among all existents, including God, so the cause of visibility applies to God, proving that God can be seen. Two marginal notes clarify: (1) The 'cause' here means the efficient cause (mu'aththir), and the invalidity of explaining one effect by different efficient causes does not apply if the causes are not efficient. (2) Non-existence can be a condition (shart) even if not part of the efficient cause, so the objection against it is addressed.

Key Terms
العلة المشتركة
الوجود
الحدث
العدم
صحة الرؤية
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think contingency (huduth) could be the common cause of visibility because it is a common attribute of created things.
Correction: The author shows that contingency includes non-existence, which cannot be part of a cause because causation is a positive attribute. Therefore only existence qualifies.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject contingency (huduth) as the common cause of visibility?
A) Because contingency is not shared between substance and accident.
B) Because contingency includes non-existence, which cannot be part of a cause.
C) Because visibility is only possible for eternal beings.
D) Because contingency is a negative attribute.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of vision is rooted in existence itself, which is common to all existents including God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that existence itself is the ground for visibility. God exists, yet you do not see Him now. Is your certainty in His reality as strong as your certainty in the chair you sit on? If not, what would it take for you to live with that same immediate conviction of His presence?
?
Contingency was dismissed as a cause because it includes non-existence. In your own life, do you let the 'non-existence' of doubt, forgetfulness, or sin dilute your awareness of God's constant existence? What one practice could you begin today to root your consciousness in His pure existence rather than in fleeting circumstances?
?
If existence is the common cause of visibility, then everything that exists shares a fundamental connection with God—including you. How does that change the way you see yourself and others? Will you begin to treat every existent thing as a sign of the One whose existence makes it visible?

The Cause of Vision's Possibility is its Object
Objection and answer about the cause of vision's possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that possibility does not need a cause is answered by distinguishing the meaning of 'cause' and by proving the object of vision must exist.

Core Idea
The 'cause' of vision's possibility is not an efficient cause but the object seen.

The second objection against the necessity of a cause for vision is that possibility is non-existent and thus requires no cause. The answer reframes the debate: the cause here means the object of vision, not an efficient cause. Moreover, we know necessarily that the object must exist, as a non-existent cannot be seen.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the answer
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Then read the translation. Notice that the objector claims possibility is non-existent and therefore needs no cause.
The objector is challenging the need for a cause at all.
2
Distinguish between the two answers
The response has two parts: a dialectical answer (counter-objection) and an analytical answer (redefinition). Identify each.
The dialectical answer refers to proofs that possibility is existent, while the analytical answer reinterprets 'cause'.
3
Understand the redefinition of 'cause'
The analytical answer clarifies that 'cause of the possibility of vision' means the object of vision, not an efficient cause. This reframes the entire discussion.
Now the need for a cause becomes obvious: vision requires an object.
4
Recognize the necessary existence of the object
The passage concludes that we know necessarily that the object of vision must be existent, because a non-existent cannot be seen.
This is a self-evident truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني من وجوه الاعتراض: لا نسلم احتياج الصحة إلى علة لأنها الإمكان والإمكان عدمي لما تقدم في باب الإمكان، والعدمي لا حاجة به إلى علة. والجواب: جدلاً المعارضة بما سبق فيه أي في باب الإمكان الأدلة الدالة على كونه وجودياً. والجواب تحقيقاً: أن المراد بعلة صحة الرؤية عند الآمدي ما يمكن أن يتعلق به الرؤية لا ما يؤثر في الصحة، واحتياج الصحة سواء كانت وجودية أو عدمية إلى العلة بمعنى متعلق الرؤية ضروري. ونعلم أيضاً بالضرورة أنه أي متعلق الرؤية أمر موجود لأن المعدوم لا تصح رؤيته قطعاً.
The second objection: We do not grant that the possibility needs a cause, because it is possibility, and possibility is non-existent, as established in the chapter on possibility, and the non-existent has no need for a cause. The answer: Dialectically, by counter-objection with what was previously presented in the chapter on possibility, that the proofs indicate it is existent. And analytically, the intended meaning of 'cause of the possibility of vision' with al-Amidi is that which can be the object of vision, not that which affects the possibility. And the need of possibility, whether it is existent or non-existent, for a cause in the sense of the object of vision is necessary. And we also know necessarily that the object of vision is an existent thing, because the non-existent absolutely cannot be seen.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against the claim that vision requires a cause. The objector says that the possibility of vision does not need a cause because possibility is non-existent, and the non-existent does not require a cause. The answer is twofold. First, dialectically, they counter by referring to proofs that show possibility is existent. Second, analytically, they clarify that by 'cause of the possibility of vision' what is meant is the object of vision, not something that produces possibility. Whether possibility is existent or not, it needs a cause in the sense of an object. Moreover, we know necessarily that the object of vision must be existent, because a non-existent cannot be seen.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of vision requires an existent object, and 'cause' here means the object of vision, not an efficient cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in the unseen (al-ghayb). But this lesson insists that genuine vision requires an existent object. What ‘invisible’ things do you treat as real without evidence, and how might this be self-deception?
?
When you picture your own shortcomings or imagined futures, do you see them as truly existent or as non-existent illusions? How does the necessity of a real object for vision challenge the way you dwell on fears or fantasies?
?
If the non-existent absolutely cannot be seen, then what are you looking at when you fixate on worldly worries that have no tangible reality? What one concrete existent reality (like Allah's promise or a present blessing) will you deliberately turn your sight to today?

Objection on Common Attributes and Incomplete Answer
Fourth objection listing many common universals, with partial answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection regarding common attributes between substance and accident, and why the author's answer is incomplete.

Core Idea
Many universals are common, challenging the limited list of existence and origination.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the fourth objection that many universal concepts are shared between substance and accident beyond just existence and origination. The objection highlights that possibility, quiddity, and others are also common. The author's answer begins but is cut off, leaving us to consider how the argument might continue.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it starts with 'The fourth objection' and lists several universals.
The answer is incomplete, so focus on the objection itself.
2
Identify the universals mentioned
List all the universals that the objector claims are common between substance and accident: existence, origination, possibility, quiddity, being-known, etc.
These are called 'universal concepts' (al-mafāhīm al-ʿāmma).
3
Reflect on the incomplete answer
The answer begins with 'We have already shown that the object...' but cuts off. Consider what the author might have intended to say to counter the objection.
This passage ends with a dangling answer, prompting further thought.

The author starts his answer by saying 'We have already shown that the object...' but the text cuts off. Presumably he goes on to state that the object of vision is the common identity, which encompasses these universals but is not restricted to them.

The Fourth Objection: Only Existence is Common
Fourth objection and its answer regarding commonality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why only existence qualifies as the common object of vision between substance and accident, and why other universals like possibility do not.

Core Idea
Only existence is specific enough to be the object of vision.

This passage presents the fourth objection to the argument that the commonality between substance and accident is existence and temporality. The objector claims that possibility and other universals are also common, but the answer shows why these are invalid. As you read, notice how the answer refines the condition that the object of vision must be specific to the existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Identify the additional commonalities proposed by the objector beyond existence and temporality.
Possibility, being-mentioned, being-known are listed.
2
Read the answer
Note the key condition: the object of vision must be specific to the existent. Determine why possibility and other universals fail this condition.
3
Understand the conclusion
Trace how only absolute existence remains as the object, after eliminating temporality and particularities.
The answer also rules out temporality as a distinct possibility.

الرابع من وجوه الاعتراض: لا نسلم أن المشترك بينهما أي الجوهر والعرض ليس إلا الوجود والحدوث فإن الإمكان أيضاً مشترك بينهما وكذا المذكورية والمعلومية وسائر المفهومات العامة. والجواب: أنا قد بينا أن متعلق الرؤية الذي فسرنا به علة الصحة هو ما يختص بالموجود وإلا لصح رؤية المعدوم والإمكان ليس كذلك لشموله الموجود والمعدوم وكذا سائر المفهومات الشاملة لهما فلا يصح شيء منهما متعلقا للرؤية. وما لا يعلم لا يكون متعلق الرؤية لأن متعلقها يجب أن يكون معلوماً لكونه مدركاً بالبصر. والذي نعلمه فيهما أي في الجوهر والعرض الموجودين خصوصية كل منهما وقد أبطلنا تعلق الرؤية بها ولم يبق لتعلقها إلا المشترك بينهما وهو الوجود إما مع خصوصية بها يمتاز كل منهما عن القديم وإنما هو مطلق الحدوث، وقد أبطلناه أيضاً وإما بدون ذلك وهو مطلق الوجود وبذلك يتم المطلوب.
The fourth of the aspects of objection: We do not concede that what is common between them, i.e., substance and accident, is only existence and temporality, for possibility is also common between them, as well as being-mentioned, being-known, and all the universal concepts. The answer: We have already shown that the object of vision, by which we explained the cause of validity, is that which is specific to the existent; otherwise, the vision of the non-existent would be valid. But possibility is not like that, because it encompasses the existent and the non-existent, as do all the other concepts that include both, so none of them is valid as an object of vision. And what is not known cannot be the object of vision, because its object must be known since it is perceived by sight. And what we know in them, i.e., in the existent substance and accident, is the particularity of each. We have already invalidated the attachment of vision to them, and nothing remains for its attachment except what is common between them, which is existence—either along with a particularity by which each is distinguished from the eternal, which is absolute temporality, and we have also invalidated that; or without that, which is absolute existence. And thereby the desired conclusion is achieved.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection and its answer. The objection argues that the commonality between substance and accident is not limited to existence and temporality but includes possibility, being-mentioned, being-known, and other universal concepts. The answer responds that the object of vision, which is the cause of validity, must be specific to the existent, otherwise the non-existent could be seen. Possibility and other universals apply to both existent and non-existent, so they are invalid as objects. Additionally, the object must be known, and we only know the particularities of existent substances and accidents, which we have already shown are not the objects. Thus only existence remains, either absolute temporality (which is also invalidated) or absolute existence, which is the correct object.

Correction: They are not, because they encompass both existent and non-existent, whereas the object of vision must be specific to the existent.

C) Because it is not known by sight

The only valid common object of vision for substance and accident is absolute existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objection lists many common concepts (like possibility, being-known) that seem reasonable. When you think about what connects you to others, do you tend to settle for surface-level commonalities (shared interests, knowledge) instead of the deeper reality of existence? What would change if you prioritized seeing every person's sheer existence as the most fundamental bond?
?
The argument strips away everything except absolute existence as the valid object of vision. In your own life, what are you 'seeing' in people and things that is not truly essential—qualities, roles, possibilities? How can you train yourself to look beyond accidents and recognize the raw existence that points to the Creator?
?
If the object of vision must be specific to the existent, then your vision itself is meant for what genuinely 'is'. Are you using your sight to dwell on illusions, possibilities, and fleeting attributes? Commit to one concrete change this week: either a distraction you will remove from your focus or a new way of seeing that acknowledges existence as a divine gift.

Objection and answer: waiting for blessing causes grief
Objection that waiting for blessing is grief, and its answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that waiting for a blessing might not cause grief in the afterlife, and how the answer defends the necessity of grief for glad tidings.

Core Idea
Glad tidings require the promised thing to be normally free of grief.

This passage addresses a subtle objection about the nature of waiting for divine blessings. Could Allah remove the grief of waiting in the afterlife? The answer explains why the very concept of glad tidings demands that the promised blessing be normally associated with joy, not grief, even if miracles are possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the objection and answer.
The passage is concise but packed.
2
Identify the objection
Find the two parts of the objection: grief as Allah's act, and the condition of certainty.
3
Analyze the answer
Notice how the answer shifts from possibility to the nature of glad tidings.
The answer uses an analogy about fire.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider why theological arguments about the afterlife rely on normal patterns, not sheer possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (والجواب : أن انتظار النعمة غم) اعترض عليه بأن حصول الغم عند الانتظار من فعل الله تعالى، فيجوز أن لا يفعل في الآخرة لأنها دار خوارق العادات على أن الانتظار إنما يفيد الغم إذا لم يكن الوصول إلى المنتظر مقطوعاً ورد بأن الآية مذكورة في معرض البشارة وذلك يستدعي كون المذكور من جنس ما لا يقارنه بالغم في العادة حتى يشتد الرغبة عنده ألا يرى أنه تعالى لم يرغب عباده إلا بأشياء التي اعتقدوا كونها لذيذة ولم يرغبهم الدخول في النيران وإن كان يجوز عقلاً أن يجعلها عليهم برداً وسلاماً، والانتظار لا يخلو في العادة عن نوع غم لمداخلة الوهم وإن كان الواعد صدوقاً فلا يناسب البشارة .
His saying: '(The answer: that waiting for the blessing is grief).' It was objected against it that the occurrence of grief during waiting is an act of Allah, so it is possible that He does not cause it in the afterlife because it is the abode of miraculous events. Moreover, waiting only occasions grief if arrival at the awaited thing is not certain. He replied: The verse is mentioned in the context of glad tidings, which requires that the mentioned thing be of a kind not normally accompanied by grief, so that desire becomes intense. Do you not see that Allah has only encouraged His servants with things they consider pleasant, and did not encourage them to enter fire even if it were intellectually possible for Him to make it cool and safe for them. And waiting is not normally free from some grief due to the intervention of imagination, even if the promiser is truthful, so it does not befit glad tidings.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the claim that waiting for a blessing is grief. The objection argues that grief during waiting is an act of Allah, and He could refrain from creating it in the afterlife, which is a realm of miraculous events. Further, waiting only causes grief if the awaited outcome is uncertain. The answer counters that the verse (about waiting for Allah) is presented as glad tidings, so the thing promised must normally be free of grief to arouse desire. Otherwise, Allah would have encouraged entering fire if it were possible, but He did not. Normally, waiting involves some grief due to the imagination's interference, even if the promiser is truthful, so it does not suit glad tidings.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Waiting for a blessing might not cause grief in the afterlife because Allah could prevent it, so it is suitable for glad tidings.
Correction: The answer shows that regardless of possibility, the normal association of waiting with grief makes it unsuitable for glad tidings, as glad tidings require something normally pleasurable.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Glad tidings must involve something normally pleasurable, not something normally associated with grief, even if miraculous alteration is possible.

?

Distant Objects Show Vision Is Not Necessitated by Conditions
Al-Jurjani refutes the necessity of seeing God by using the example of distant objects appearing small.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fulfillment of the eight conditions for physical vision does not necessitate seeing God, as demonstrated by the example of distant large objects appearing small.

Core Idea
Meeting all conditions for vision does not guarantee seeing—distant objects prove that.

In this passage, al-Jurjani tackles a logical objection: if the conditions for vision are met, then seeing God would be necessary. He responds by pointing to a common experience—a large distant object appears small because only some parts are seen, even though all parts meet the conditions. This shows that conditions are necessary but not sufficient, and thus the objection fails. Let us follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector argues that since two conditions (sound sense and healthy vision) are always present, seeing God is necessary. Read the first part of the passage to grasp this.
The objector relies on the eight conditions for physical vision.
2
Examine al-Jurjani's counterexample
Al-Jurjani points out that a large distant object is seen small—only some parts are visible. All parts equally fulfill the conditions, yet not all are seen. This disproves necessity.
This is a direct empirical refutation.
3
Consider the optical objection
An opponent might argue that geometry explains why only the center is seen (radial lines make the center closer). Read that part carefully.
The opponent uses a triangle diagram.
4
See al-Jurjani's rebuttal
Al-Jurjani responds that even if the distance disparity is large, if distance were the cause, moving the whole object away would make it invisible—but it remains visible, so distance is not the cause. Thus all parts remain equal in conditions, yet not all are seen.
This strengthens the conclusion that conditions do not necessitate vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الأول : في الرؤية والكلام نعقل من هذه الشرائط في حق رؤية الله تعالى إلا سلامة الحاسة وصحة الرؤية لكون الست البواقي منها مختصة بالأجسام وهما أي الشرطان المعقولان في رؤيته حاصلان الآن فوجب حصول رؤيته. والجواب : عن هذه الشبهة إما أولاً : فهو أنا لا نسلم وجوب الرؤية عند اجتماع الشروط الثمانية وذلك لأن دليلكم وإن دل عليه لكن عندنا ما ينفيه لأنا نرى الجسم الكبير من البعيد صغيراً وما ذلك إلا لأنا نرى بعض أجزائه دون البعض مع تساوي الكل في حصول الشرائط. فظهر أنه لا تجب الرؤية عند اجتماعها لا يقال : يتصل بطرفي المرئي من العين خطان شعاعيان كساقي مثلث قاعدته سطح المرئي ويخرج منها أي من العين إلى وسطه خط قائم عليه أي على سطحه يقسم ذلك الخط المثلث المذكور إلى مثلثين قائمتين الزاوية الواقعة عن جنبتي الخط القائم فيكون الخط الوسط وتراً لكل واحدة من الزاويتين الحادتين وكل من الطرفين وتر الزاوية قائمة ووتر القائمة في المثلث أطول من وتر الحادة فلم تكن أجزاء المرئي متساوية في القرب والبعد بالنسبة إلى الرائي بل يكون وسط المرئي أقرب إليه من طرفيه، فجاز أن يرى الوسط وحده بدون الطرفين لأنا نقول : نفرض هذا التفاوت الذي ذكرتموه في هذه الخطوط ذراعاً فلو كان عدم رؤية الطرفين لأجل البعد فإذا فرض أنه بعد المرئي يقدر ذلك البعد الذي لطرفيه وجب أن لا يرى أصلاً ، وإذا يرى فهذا البعد لا أثر له في عدم الرؤية. فتكون الأجزاء كلها مع ذلك التفاوت متساوية في حصول شرائط الرؤية، وبعضها غير مرئي فلا تجب الرؤية مع حصولها.
The Fifth Observatory - The First Objective: On Vision and Speech. We understand from these conditions regarding the vision of God Almighty except soundness of the sense and soundness of vision because the remaining six are specific to bodies. And the two conditions understood concerning His vision are present now, so the occurrence of His vision is necessary. And the answer to this specious argument: first, it is that we do not admit the necessity of vision when the eight conditions are met, because your proof, even if it indicates it, but we have something that negates it: for we see a large body from a distance as small, and that is only because we see some of its parts without others, while all parts are equal in the acquisition of the conditions. So it appears that vision is not necessary when they are met. It cannot be said: two radial lines connect from the eye to the two ends of the visible object like the legs of a triangle whose base is the surface of the visible object, and from it—i.e., from the eye—a line perpendicular to it—i.e., to its surface—goes out to its center, dividing that line the mentioned triangle into two right triangles with the angle on either side of the perpendicular line, so the middle line becomes a hypotenuse for each of the two acute angles, and each of the two ends is the hypotenuse of a right angle, and the hypotenuse of the right angle in the triangle is longer than the hypotenuse of the acute angle, so the parts of the visible object are not equal in nearness and distance to the viewer, but rather the center of the visible object is closer to him than its two ends, so it is possible that the center alone is seen without the two ends. For we say: we assume this disparity which you mentioned in these lines as a cubit. So if the non-vision of the two ends were due to distance, then if it is assumed that the distance of the visible object is estimated by that distance of its two ends, it would necessarily not be seen at all. But if it is seen, then this distance has no effect on non-vision. Thus all the parts, despite that disparity, are equal in obtaining the conditions of vision, yet some parts are not visible, so vision is not necessary when the conditions are met.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is an objection and response concerning the necessity of seeing God. The author begins by stating the problem: from the eight conditions for physical vision, only soundness of the sense and soundness of vision are relevant for seeing God, since the other six apply only to bodies. Since these two conditions are present now, it would seem that seeing God is necessary. The author then presents a response: he does not admit the necessity of vision when all eight conditions are met, because we observe that a large distant object appears small—we see only some of its parts, not all, even though all parts equally fulfill the conditions. This shows that meeting the conditions does not guarantee vision. He anticipates an objection: the objector says that an optical ray diagram explains why only the center is seen—two radial lines form a triangle with the visible object, and a perpendicular line from the eye to the center creates two right triangles. The hypotenuse (center line) is shorter, so the center is nearer and seen alone. The author's reply: even if we assume the disparity in distances is as much as a cubit, if the non-vision of the ends were due to distance, then if the object were moved away by that same distance, it would not be seen at all—but it is seen, so that distance does not prevent vision. Therefore all parts are equal in fulfilling conditions, yet some are not seen, so vision is not necessitated by the conditions alone.

سلامة الحاسة
صحة الرؤية
وجوب الرؤية
الخطوط الشعاعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If all eight conditions for physical vision are met, then vision must occur.
Correction: The passage shows through the example of a distant large object that even when conditions are met, vision may not occur (only some parts are seen). Therefore conditions are necessary but not sufficient.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objection and response in this passage?
A) That seeing God is impossible because the conditions are not met.
B) That the eight conditions for vision are necessary but not sufficient, as shown by distant objects.
C) That the two relevant conditions are not present now, so God cannot be seen.
D) That the objector's argument about radial lines proves vision is necessary.

Key Takeaway
The fulfillment of all conditions for vision does not necessitate seeing God, as the example of distant objects shows that vision may fail even when conditions are met.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever presumed that because you diligently perform the outward acts of worship, you should automatically experience a profound sense of God's presence or vision in your heart? When that doesn't happen, do you feel disillusioned? How does this passage's lesson—that conditions do not necessitate vision—challenge you to release your expectations and trust God's free self-disclosure?
?
Al-Jurjani uses a physical example to expose a logical flaw. Is there a belief you hold about God that relies on a human analogy or assumed necessity? Are you willing to let go of that assumption and accept that God is not bound by your reasoning? What would it cost you to do so?

Refuting the Impression Theory of Vision via Indivisible Parts
Jurjani refutes the impression theory of vision by showing it contradicts the nature of indivisible parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani uses the concept of indivisible parts to refute the impression theory of vision and why this refutation is logically necessary.

Core Idea
If visible objects consist of indivisible parts, size variation in vision cannot occur by imprinting.

The impression theory of vision claims that we see objects by their image being imprinted on the eye at a specific angle, and that apparent size varies with the angle. Al-Jurjani challenges this by appealing to a fundamental premise of kalām atomism: the existence of indivisible parts. By analyzing what would happen if all or some parts were seen, he shows that the impression theory leads to absurdities. This lesson walks through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the impression theory
Read the first part of the passage where the theory is presented: the image is imprinted in the apex angle of a cone, and size varies with angle width.
2
Identify the assumption of indivisible parts
Notice that the author introduces 'the composition of indivisible parts' as the basis for his refutation.
This is a key premise in kalām atomism.
3
Follow the refutation's logic
The author enumerates possibilities: if all parts are seen, object appears as is; if parts appear smaller, indivisibility is violated; if larger, object appears double, etc. Each leads to contradiction.
Pay attention to the logical 'or' structure.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The only consistent conclusion is that variation is due to seeing some parts, not all, which refutes the impression theory's explanation of size change.
This completes the counter-argument against the necessity of vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن القائلين بالانطباع ذهبوا إلى أن صورة المرئي إنما ترتسم من الرطوبة الجليدية في زاوية رأس مخروط متوهم قاعدته عند المرئي، وإن اختلافه بالصغر والكبر في الرؤية إنما هو بحسب ضيق تلك الزاوية وسعتها ولهذا إذا قرب المرئي في الغاية أو بعد في الغاية صارت الزاوية لسعتها في الغاية حال القرب أو لضيقها في الغاية حال البعد كالمعدوم فانعدمت الرؤية حينئذ لعدم انطباع الصورة قال المصنف : وضعفه ظاهر بناء على تركب الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ إذ على هذا التقدير إن رأى الأجزاء كلها وجب أن يرى الجسم كما هو في الواقع سواء كان قريبا أو بعيدا وذلك لأن الرؤية لك منها أو بعضها أصغر مما هو عليه توجب الانقسام فيما لا يتجزئ لثبوت ما هو أصغر ورؤيته أي رؤية كل من الأجزاء أكبر مما هو عليه بمثل أو بأزيد منه توجب أن لا يرى إلا ضعفاً أو أكبر من ذلك وهو باطل قطعاً ورؤيته أكبر بأقل من مثل الانقسام ورؤية بعضها على ما هو عليه بعضها أكبر بمثل توجب ترجيحا بلا مرجح فوجب أن يرى الكل على حاله فلا تفاوت حينئذ بالصغر والكبر، فتعين أن يكون التفاوت بحسب رؤية بعض دون بعض فتمت معارضتنا لدليلهم على وجوب الرؤية عند اجتماع شرائطها
For the proponents of impression went to the view that the image of the visible object is imprinted from the crystalline moisture in the angle of the apex of a hypothetical cone whose base is at the visible object, and that its variation in smallness and largeness in vision is according to the narrowness and width of that angle. And for this reason, if the visible object is extremely near or extremely far, the angle becomes, due to its extreme width in the case of nearness or its extreme narrowness in the case of distance, like non-existent, so vision ceases then due to the failure of the image to be imprinted. The author said: Its weakness is apparent based on the composition of indivisible parts, for on this assumption, if all the parts are seen, it would be necessary that the body be seen as it is in reality, whether near or far. That is because seeing each of them or some of them smaller than it actually is would necessitate division in what is indivisible due to the existence of what is smaller; and seeing it—i.e., seeing each of the parts—larger than it actually is by a like amount or more would necessitate that it not be seen except as double or more, which is absolutely false; and seeing it larger by less than a like amount would require division; and seeing some of them as they are and some larger by a like amount would require preferment without a preponderant. Therefore it is necessary that the whole be seen as it is, so there is no variation then in smallness and largeness. Thus it is determined that the variation is due to seeing some parts without others. So our counter-argument against their proof for the necessity of vision when its conditions are met is complete.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the impression theory of vision (al-intiba') using the concept of indivisible parts (ajza' allati la tatajaza'). The proponents of impression hold that the image of a visible object is imprinted at a specific angle in the eye's crystalline moisture, and the apparent size varies with the width of that angle—very near or very far objects cause the angle to be extremely wide or narrow, preventing imprinting and thus vision. The author (al-Jurjani) points out the weakness: if we assume the visible object is composed of indivisible parts (atoms), then when seeing, if all parts are seen, the object must appear exactly as it is. If some parts appear smaller, that would require dividing an indivisible part, which is impossible. If they appear larger, that would imply the object appears double or more, which is false. Any other combination leads to unjustified preference. Therefore, the only consistent conclusion is that the whole object is seen as it is, with no variation in size. Consequently, the apparent smallness of distant objects must be due to seeing only some parts, not the whole. This completes the counter-argument against the claim that vision is necessary when all conditions are met.

Key Terms
الانطباع
الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ
الرطوبة الجليدية
زاوية رأس مخروط
وجوب الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impression theory can explain size variation in vision even if objects are made of indivisible parts.
Correction: The impression theory requires that parts appear smaller or larger, which would require dividing indivisible parts or seeing double, both impossible under atomism.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw the author finds in the impression theory of vision?
A) It denies the existence of indivisible parts.
B) It requires that an object's parts be divisible, which is impossible if they are indivisible.
C) It claims that the angle becomes non-existent, which is physically impossible.
D) It fails to explain why nearby objects appear larger.

Key Takeaway
The impression theory of vision is incompatible with the concept of indivisible parts because any variation in perceived size would require dividing the indivisible, thus the theory must be false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that our perception of size is not a direct imprint but depends on which parts are seen. How often do you base judgments on incomplete perception, assuming you see the whole picture? What would it look like to actively seek the missing parts in a situation you feel certain about?
?
Jurjani uses logical reasoning to dismantle a seemingly intuitive theory. When have you clung to an idea because it felt intuitive, even though reason pointed to its flaws? Will you commit to testing your cherished beliefs with the same rigorous logic?
?
The impression theory fails because it cannot account for variation under atomism. In your spiritual life, are there practices or understandings that feel right but actually conflict with deeper principles of tawḥīd? How can you use rational scrutiny to refine your worship and beliefs?
